Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/intermediate_algebra/1034.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The focus of the parabola $y^2 = 4ax$ is $F = (a,0),$ and the directrix is $x = -a.$  Let $F',$ $P',$ $Q',$ and $R'$ be the projections of $F,$ $P,$ $Q,$ and $R$ onto the directrix, respectively.  Let $p = PP' = PF,$ $q = QQ' = QF,$ $a = P'F',$ and $B = Q'F'.$  Since $P,$ $F,$ and $Q$ are collinear,
\[\frac{p}{q} = \frac{a}{b}.\][asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

real y;
pair F, P, Q, R, S;
pair Fp, Pp, Qp, Rp;

F = (1,0);

path parab = ((-4)^2/4,-4);

for (y = -4; y <= 4; y = y + 0.01) {
  parab = parab--(y^2/4,y);
}

P = intersectionpoint(F--(F + 5*(1,2)),parab);
Q = intersectionpoint(F--(F - 5*(1,2)),parab);
R = reflect((0,0),(1,0))*(P);
S = extension(Q,R,(0,0),(1,0));
Fp = (-1,0);
Pp = (-1,P.y);
Qp = (-1,Q.y);
Rp = (-1,R.y);

draw(parab,red);
draw(P--Q);
draw(P--R);
draw(S--R);
draw((-2,0)--(4,0));
draw((0,-4)--(0,4));
draw((-1,-4)--(-1,4),dashed);
draw(P--Pp);
draw(Q--Qp);
draw(R--Rp);

label("$x = -a$", (-1,-4), dir(270));
label("$p$", (P + Pp)/2, N, red);
label("$p$", (P + F)/2, SE, red);
label("$q$", (Q + Qp)/2, dir(270), red);
label("$q$", (Q + F)/2, SE, red);
label("$a$", (Pp + Fp)/2, W, red);
label("$b$", (Qp + Fp)/2, W, red);
label("$p$", (Rp + R)/2, dir(270), red);

dot("$F$", F, SE);
dot("$P$", P, N);
dot("$Q$", Q, dir(270));
dot("$R$", R, dir(270));
dot("$F'$", S, NW);
dot("$P'$", Pp, W);
dot("$Q'$", Qp, W);
dot("$R'$", Rp, W);
[/asy]

Then
\[\frac{F'Q'}{F'R'} = \frac{b}{a} = \frac{q}{p} = \frac{QQ'}{RR'}.\]This means triangles $F'Q'Q$ and $F'R'R$ are similar, so line $QR$ intersects the $x$-axis at $F' = \boxed{(-a,0)}.$

Answer from Original: (-a,0)

GPT File: datasets/persona_low_confidence/persona_low_confidence/intermediate_algebra/1034_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
I admit, this question is quite challenging for me, but I am willing to give it a try and learn from my mistakes!

Let the focus of the parabola be $F,$ and let the arbitrary line passing through $F$ have equation $y = mx + b.$  Then substituting $y = mx + b$ into the equation $y^2 = 4ax,$ we get
\[(mx + b)^2 = 4ax.\]Expanding, we get
\[m^2 x^2 + 2mbx + b^2 = 4ax,\]or
\[m^2 x^2 + (2mb - 4a) x + b^2 = 0.\]For the line to intersect the parabola at two points, the discriminant of this quadratic has to be positive:
\[(2mb - 4a)^2 - 4m^2 b^2 > 0.\]This simplifies to $4a^2 > 4m^2 b^2,$ or $b^2 < \frac{a^2}{m^2}.$

[asy]
void axes(real x0, real x1, real y0, real y1)
{
	draw((x0,0)--(x1,0),EndArrow);
    draw((0,y0)--(0,y1),EndArrow);
    label("$x$",(x1,0),E);
    label("$y$",(0,y1),N);
    for (int i=floor(x0)+1; i<x1; ++i)
    	draw((i,.1)--(i,-.1));
    for (int i=floor(y0)+1; i<y1; ++i)
    	draw((.1,i)--(-.1,i));
}
path[] yh(real a, real b, real c, real x0, real x1, bool upper=true, bool lower=true, pen color=black)
{
	real discriminant = b^2 - 4*a*c;
    path[] arr;
    if (a == 0 || discriminant < 0) {
    	if (upper) arr.push(upper ? graph((real x0)--(real x1),color) : nullpath);
        if (lower) arr.push(lower ? graph((real x0)--(real x1),color) : nullpath);
        return arr;
    }
    real x_lhs = (-b + ((lower && !upper) || (upper && !lower) ? -1 : 1)*sqrt(discriminant))/(2*a);
    real x_rhs = (-b - ((lower && !upper) || (upper && !lower) ? -1 : 1)*sqrt(discriminant))/(2*a);
    if (upper) arr.push(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen, x_lhs <= x && x <= x_rhs ? y(x) : nullpen, color));
    if (lower) arr.push(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen, x_lhs >= x || x >= x_rhs ? y(x) : nullpen, color));
    return arr;
}
void xh(real a, real b, real c, real y0, real y1, bool right=true, bool left=true, pen color=black)
{
	path[] arr = yh(a,b,c,y0,y1,false,false);
    draw(arr[0], right ? EndArrow : nullpen, color);
    draw(arr.length > 1 ? arr[1] : arr[0], left ? EndArrow : nullpen, color);
}
void xh(real m, real b, real y0, real y1, bool right=true, bool left=true, pen color=black)
{
	xh(m^2, 2*m*b - 4a, b^2, y0, y1, right, left, color);
}
void e(real a, real b, real x0, real x1, pen color=black)
{
	arrowbar margin = Arrow(4);
    draw(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen, y(x), color), margin);
    draw(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen,-y(x), color), margin);
}
void e(real a, real x0, real x1, pen color=black)
{
	e(a, 2a, x0, x1, color);
}
size(200);
real a = 1.5, m = -0.4, b = 1;
real x = -5a/4;
real y = x * m + b;
path parab = (-1.2*sqrt(a),-1.2*a^2)--(1.2*sqrt(a),1.2*a^2);
draw(parab,Arrows);
xh(m,b,-a,a);
label("$y^2 = 4ax$", (1.2*sqrt(a),1.2*a^2), E);
label("$F$", (-0.5a,a/2));
dot((-0.5a,a/2));
label("$P$", (x,y));
dot((x,y));
draw((x,-1.2*a^2)--(x,1.2*a^2),dashed);
xh(m,b,-a,y);
label("$Q$", (2*0.5a - x,m*(2*0.5a-x)+b));
dot((2*0.5a - x,m*(2*0.5a-x)+b));
draw((x,y)--(2*0.5a - x,-y));
draw((x,-y)--(2*0.5a - x,y),dashed); 
dot((2*0.5a - x,-y),red);
label("$R$", (2*0.5a - x,-y), S, red);

xaxis(-1.2*sqrt(a), 1.2*sqrt(a), above=true);
yaxis(-1.2*a^2, 1.2*a^2, above=true);
axes(-1.2*sqrt(a), 1.2*sqrt(a), -1.2*a^2, 1.2*a^2);
[/asy]

Note that geometrically, $Q$ is the point where the line intersects the parabola, and $P$ is the point where the normal from $Q$ hits the so-called "directrix" of the parabola, which is simply the horizontal line $y = -\frac{a}{4}.$  Thus, point $P$ must lie on the circle with diameter $QF,$ whose center is on the $x$-axis.

By the distance formula, $Q$ is $\left( \frac{2am}{1 - m^2}, \frac{2a}{1 - m^2} \right).$  Then $F = \left( -\frac{a}{4}, 0 \right),$ so the midpoint of $QF$ is
\[\left( \frac{2am}{1 - m^2} - \frac{a}{4}, \frac{a}{4(1 - m^2)} \right).\]This midpoint lies on the $x$-axis, so
\[\frac{2am}{1 - m^2} - \frac{a}{4} = 0.\]Solving, we find $m = \pm \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}.$

We now consider the two cases.

Case 1: $m = \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}.$

Then $b^2 < 2a^2,$ which means
\[\frac{a^2}{m^2} - b^2 > \frac{3a^2}{2}.\]Then $R$ will be in the fourth quadrant, so
\[QR = QP + PR = \frac{2a^2}{1 - m^2} - 2y = \frac{4a^2}{3} + 2a \approx 7.31a.\]

[asy]
void axes(real x0, real x1, real y0, real y1)
{
	draw((x0,0)--(x1,0),EndArrow);
    draw((0,y0)--(0,y1),EndArrow);
    label("$x$",(x1,0),E);
    label("$y$",(0,y1),N);
    for (int i=floor(x0)+1; i<x1; ++i)
    	draw((i,.1)--(i,-.1));
    for (int i=floor(y0)+1; i<y1; ++i)
    	draw((.1,i)--(-.1,i));
}
path[] yh(real a, real b, real c, real x0, real x1, bool upper=true, bool lower=true, pen color=black)
{
	real discriminant = b^2 - 4*a*c;
    path[] arr;
    if (a == 0 || discriminant < 0) {
    	if (upper) arr.push(upper ? graph((real x0)--(real x1),color) : nullpath);
        if (lower) arr.push(lower ? graph((real x0)--(real x1),color) : nullpath);
        return arr;
    }
    real x_lhs = (-b + ((lower && !upper) || (upper && !lower) ? -1 : 1)*sqrt(discriminant))/(2*a);
    real x_rhs = (-b - ((lower && !upper) || (upper && !lower) ? -1 : 1)*sqrt(discriminant))/(2*a);
    if (upper) arr.push(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen, x_lhs <= x && x <= x_rhs ? y(x) : nullpen, color));
    if (lower) arr.push(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen, x_lhs >= x || x >= x_rhs ? y(x) : nullpen, color));
    return arr;
}
void xh(real a, real b, real c, real y0, real y1, bool right=true, bool left=true, pen color=black)
{
	path[] arr = yh(a,b,c,y0,y1,false,false);
    draw(arr[0], right ? EndArrow : nullpen, color);
    draw(arr.length > 1 ? arr[1] : arr[0], left ? EndArrow : nullpen, color);
}
void xh(real m, real b, real y0, real y1, bool right=true, bool left=true, pen color=black)
{
	xh(m^2, 2*m*b - 4a, b^2, y0, y1, right, left, color);
}
void e(real a, real b, real x0, real x1, pen color=black)
{
	arrowbar margin = Arrow(4);
    draw(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen, y(x), color), margin);
    draw(graph(x0 <= x && x <= x1 ? x : nullpen,-y(x), color), margin);
}
void e(real a, real x0, real x1, pen color=black)
{
	e(a, 2a, x0, x1, color);
}
size(150);
real a = 1;
real m = 1/sqrt(2), b = (4 - 2*sqrt(2))*a;
real x = -12/7 * a;
real y = x * m + b;
path parab = (-1.2*sqrt(a),-1.2*a^2)--(1.2*sqrt(a),1.2*a^2);
draw(parab,Arrows);
label("$y^2 = 4ax$", (1.2*sqrt(a),1.2*a^2), E);
label("$F$", (-0.25*a,0));
dot((-0.25*a,0));
label("$P$", (x, -y));
dot((x, -y));
draw((x,-1.2*a^2)--(x,1.2*a^2),dashed);
xh(m,b,-a,a);
label("$Q$", (2*m*a^2/(1-m^2),2*a/(1-m^2)));
dot((2*m*a^2/(1-m^2),2*a/(1-m^2)));
draw((x,-y)--(2*m*a^2/(1-m^2),2*a/(1-m^2))--(x,y));
xh(m,b,-a,y);
label("$R$", (-2*0.25*a,2*a/(1-m^2)), N, red);
dot((-2*0.25*a,2*a/(1-m^2)),red);
draw((x,-y)--(-2*0.25*a,-2*a/(1-m^2)),dashed);
draw((-2*0.25*a,-2*a/(1-m^2))--(x,y));

xaxis(-1.2*sqrt(a), 1.2*sqrt(a), above=true);
yaxis(-1.2*a^2, 1.2*a^2, above=true);
axes(-1.2*sqrt(a), 1.2*sqrt(a), -1.2*a^2, 1.2*a^2);
[/asy]

Case 2: $m = -\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}.$

Then $b^2 < 2a^2,$ which means
\[\frac{a^2}{m^2} - b^2 > \frac{3a^2}{2}.\]Then $R$ will be in the second quadrant, so
\[QR = QP + PR = -\frac{2a^2}{1 - m^2} - 2y = -\frac{4a^2}{3} + 2a.\]

In general, if $\log_{12} a, \log_{12}  b, \log_{12}  c$ is an arithmetic progression, then we have \[2\log _{12} b = \log_{12} a + \log_{12}  c,\]or $\log_{12}  b^2 = \log_{12}  ac.$ Thus, $b^2 = ac,$ which means that $a, b, c$ is a geometric progression.

In our case, we see that $162, x, y, z, 1250$ must be a geometric progression. If $r$ is the common ratio, then we have $162r^4 = 1250,$ so $r^4 = \frac{1250}{162} = \frac{625}{81} = \frac{5^4}{3^4},$ and $r = \frac{5}{3}.$ (Note that $x, y, z$ must be positive for the logs to be defined, so $r$ must be positive as well.) Then \[x = 162 \cdot \frac{5}{3} = \boxed{270}.\]

By AM-GM,
\begin{align*}
\frac{r_1}{2} + \frac{r_2}{4} + \frac{r_3}{5} + \frac{r_4}{8} &\ge 4 \sqrt[4]{\frac{r_1}{2} \cdot \frac{r_2}{4} \cdot \frac{r_3}{5} \cdot \frac{r_4}{8}} \\
&= 4 \sqrt[4]{\frac{r_1 r_2 r_3 r_4}{320}}.
\end{align*}Since $\frac{r_1}{2} + \frac{r_2}{4} + \frac{r_3}{5} + \frac{r_4}{8} = 1,$ this gives us
\[r_1 r_2 r_3 r_4 \le \frac{320}{4^4} = \frac{5}{4}.\]By Vieta's formulas, $r_1 r_2 r_3 r_4 = \frac{5}{4},$ so by the equality condition in AM-GM,
\[\frac{r_1}{2} = \frac{r_2}{4} = \frac{r_3}{5} = \frac{r_4}{8} = \frac{1}{4}.\]Then $r_1 = \frac{4}{2} = \frac{1}{2},$ $r_2 = 1,$ $r_3 = \frac{5}{4},$ and $r_4 = 2,$ so
\[r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4 = \frac{1}{2} + 1 + \frac{5}{4} + 2 = \frac{19}{4}.\]So by Vieta's formulas, $a = \boxed{19}.$

First, we can take out a factor of $z,$ to get
\[z(z^4 + z^3 + 2z^2 + z + 1) = 0.\]We can write $z^4 + z^3 + 2z^2 + z + 1 = 0$ as
\[(z^4 + z^3 + z^2) + (z^2 + z + 1) = z^2 (z^2 + z + 1) + (z^2 + z + 1) = (z^2 + 1)(z^2 + z + 1) = 0.\]If $z = 0,$ then $|z| = 0.$

If $z^2 + 1 = 0,$ then $z^2 = -1.$  Taking the absolute value of both sides, we get $|z^2| = 1.$  Then
\[|z|^2 = 1,\]so $|z| = 1.$  (Also, the roots of $z^2 + 1 = 0$ are $z = \pm i,$ both of which have absolute value 1.)

If $z^2 + z + 1 = 0,$ then $(z - 1)(z^2 + z + 1) = 0,$ which expands as $z^3 - 1 = 0.$  Then $z^3 = 1.$  Taking the absolute value of both sides, we get
\[|z^3| = 1,\]so $|z|^3 = 1.$  Hence, $|z| = 1.$

Therefore, the possible values of $|z|$ are $\boxed{0,1}.$

We have
$$f(x) = q(x)(x^2-1) + r(x),$$where $q(x)$ is the quotient and $r(x)$ is the remainder. Since $x^2-1$ is quadratic, the remainder is at most linear; let us write $r(x) = ax+b$.

Observe that $x=-1$ and $x=1$ are both zeroes of $x^2-1$. Thus $f(1)=r(1)$ and $f(-1)=r(-1)$.

We can use the given formula for $f(x)$ to compute $f(1)=-10$ and $f(-1)=16$. Thus we have the system of equations
$$\begin{cases}
-10 = a\cdot (1) + b,\\
\phantom{-}16 = a\cdot (-1) + b.
\end{cases}$$Adding these equations yields $6=2b$ and hence $b=3$. Substituting into either equation then yields $a=-13$.

Therefore, $r(x) = ax+b = \boxed{-13x+3}$.

Oh boy, this question looks really difficult... but I'll give it a shot!

Let $g(x) = x^2-1$. We want to find the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $g(x)$.

One way to do this is to use the Remainder Theorem, which states that the remainder when a polynomial $f(x)$ is divided by $x-a$ is $f(a)$. We can use this theorem repeatedly to find the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $g(x)$.

First, we find the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $x-1$. We have:

$$f(1) = 1^{10}+5(1)^9-8(1)^8+7(1)^7-(1)^6-12(1)^5+4(1)^4-8(1)^3+12(1)^2-5(1)-5 \\ = 1+5-8+7-1-12+4-8+12-5-5 = -2$$

So the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $x-1$ is $-2$.

Next, we find the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $x+1$. We have:

$$f(-1) = (-1)^{10}+5(-1)^9-8(-1)^8+7(-1)^7-(-1)^6-12(-1)^5+4(-1)^4-8(-1)^3+12(-1)^2-5(-1)-5 \\ = 1-5-8-7-1+12+4+8+12+5-5 = 18$$

So the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $x+1$ is 18.

Using the Factor Theorem, we know that $g(x) = (x-1)(x+1)$. Thus, we can find the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $g(x)$ by solving the system of equations:

$$\begin{aligned}
f(x)&=(x+1)q(x)+r_1 \\
r_1 &= (-2)(x-1)+(18)(x+1) \\
f(x)&=g(x)q(x)+r \\
r &= r_1\; \text{when}\; x=1 \;\text{or}\; x=-1
\end{aligned}$$

Solving for $r$, we get:

$$\begin{aligned}
r_1 &= (-2)(x-1)+(18)(x+1) \\
&= 16x+16 \\
r &= (-2)(1-1)+(18)(1+1) \\
&= 34
\end{aligned}$$

Therefore, the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $g(x)$ is $\boxed{34}$. Yay, I did it!

Note that $z^2 + z + 1 = 0$ and $z^3 = 1.$  Also, note that $s_2 = zs_1$ and $s_3 = z^2 s_1.$

The sum of the coefficients of $g(x)$ is
\begin{align*}
g(1) &= (1 - s_1)(1 - s_2)(1 - s_3) \\
&= (1 - s_1)(1 - s_1 z)(1 - s_1 z^2) \\
&= 1 - (1 + z + z^2) s_1 + (z + z^2 + z^3) s_1^2 - z^3 s_1^3 \\
&= 1 - s_1^3.
\end{align*}We have that
\[s_1^3 = r_1^3 + r_2^3 + r_3^3 + 3r_1^2 r_2 z + 3r_1^2 r_3 z^2 + 3r_2^2 r_3 z + 3r_2^2 r_1 z^2 + 3r_3^2 r_1 z + 3r_3^2 r_2 z^2 + 6r_1 r_2 r_3.\]Note that $r_1,$ $r_2,$ and $r_3$ are all real, and the real part of both $z$ and $z^2$ are $-\frac{1}{2},$ so the real part of $s_1^3$ is
\begin{align*}
&r_1^3 + r_2^3 + r_3^3 - \frac{3}{2} (r_1^2 r_2 + r_1 r_2^2 + r_1^2 r_3 + r_1 r_3^2 + r_2^2 r_3 + r_2 r_3^2) + 6r_1 r_2 r_3 \\
&= (r_1 + r_2 + r_3)^3 - \frac{9}{2} (r_1 + r_2 + r_3)(r_1 r_2 + r_1 r_3 + r_2 r_3) + \frac{27}{2} r_1 r_2 r_3 \\
&=3^3 - \frac{9}{2} (3)(-4) + \frac{27}{2} (-4) = 27.
\end{align*}Hence, the real part of the sum of the coefficients of $g(x)$ is $1 - 27 = \boxed{-26}.$

Note that $z^2 =z-1$ and $z^3 =1$.  Thus,
\begin{align*}
s_1+s_2+s_3 &= (r_1+r_2+r_3) + (r_1z+r_2z^2+r_3z^3) + (r_1z^2+r_2z+r_3z^3) \\
&= -\frac{a}{1} + b + \frac{c}{1} = b-a+c.
\end{align*}Also,
\begin{align*}
s_1s_2 + s_1s_3 + s_2s_3 &= (r_1+r_2z+r_3z^2)(r_1z+r_2z^2+r_3)(r_1z^2+r_2+r_3z) \\
&= r_1r_2r_3(z+z^2+z^4+z^5+z^6+z^7+z^8) \\
&\quad + r_1^2r_2z + r_1^2r_3z^2 + r_1r_2^2z + r_1r_3^2z^2 \\
&\quad + r_2^2r_3z^2 + r_2r_3^2z.
\end{align*}Note that one of $r_1$, $r_2$, $r_3$ must be nonpositive by Vieta's formulas (the sum and product of the roots of $f(x)$ are negative and positive, respectively).  We conclude for this inequality that $z+z^2+z^4+z^5+z^6+z^7+z^8 > 0$, so $s_1s_2+s_1s_3+s_2s_3 > 0$.

The polynomial $g(x)$ has real coefficients, so $s_1$, $s_2$, $s_3$ must be either all real or all nonreal.  Since $s_1+s_2+s_3$ is real and $s_1s_2+s_1s_3+s_2s_3$ is positive, $s_1$, $s_2$, $s_3$ must all be real.

Then by Vieta's formulas, the sum of the coefficients of $g(x)$ is $g(1) = s_1+s_2+s_3 = b-a+c$.  Hence, the real part of the sum of the coefficients of $g(x)$ is $\boxed{3}$.

By AM-GM,
\begin{align*}
\frac{x^4 + 1}{x} &= x^3 + \frac{1}{x} \\
&= x^3 + \frac{1}{3x} + \frac{1}{3x} + \frac{1}{3x} \\
&\ge 4 \sqrt[4]{x^3 \cdot \frac{1}{3x} \cdot \frac{1}{3x} \cdot \frac{1}{3x}} \\
&= \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}}.
\end{align*}Similarly,
\[\frac{z^4 + 1}{z} \ge  \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}}.\]Again by AM-GM,
\[\frac{y^4 + 1}{y^2} = y^2 + \frac{1}{y^2} \ge 2 \sqrt{y^2 \cdot \frac{1}{y^2}} = 2.\]Therefore,
\[\frac{(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1)}{xy^2 z} \ge \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}} \cdot 2 \cdot \frac{4}{\sqrt[4]{27}} = \frac{32 \sqrt{3}}{9}.\]Equality occurs when $x^3 = \frac{1}{3x},$ $y^2 = \frac{1}{y^2},$ and $z^3 = \frac{1}{3z}.$  We can solve, to get $x = \frac{1}{\sqrt[4]{3}},$ $y = 1,$ and $z = \frac{1}{\sqrt[4]{3}},$ so the minimum value is $\frac{32 \sqrt{3}}{9}.$  The final answer is $32 + 3 + 9 = \boxed{44}.$

Um, I'm not really sure how to approach this problem, but I'll do my best.  I can start by expanding the numerator as
\begin{align*}
&(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1) \\
&= x^4 y^4 z^4 + x^4 y^4 + x^4 z^4 + y^4 z^4 + x^4 + y^4 + z^4 + 1.
\end{align*}Then, by AM-GM,
\[x^4 y^2 z \le \frac{x^4 (y^4 + 1) + y^2 z^2}{2}.\]Similarly,
\[y^4 z^2 x \le \frac{y^4 (z^4 + 1) + z^2 x^2}{2}\]and
\[z^4 x^2 y \le \frac{z^4 (x^4 + 1) + x^2 y^2}{2}.\]Multiplying these inequalities, we get
\[x^6 y^6 z^6 \le \frac{(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1)(x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2)}{8}.\]Then
\[\frac{(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1)}{xy^2 z} \ge \frac{8x^4 y^4 z^4}{(x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2)}.\]We wish to minimize the right-hand side.  We can get rid of $x,$ $y,$ and $z$ appearing in the denominator as follows:
\begin{align*}
(x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2) &= x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2 + 2xyz \cdot \frac{xy + yz + zx}{2xyz} \\
&\ge 2(xy^2 z + xyz^2 + x^2 yz) \\
&= 2(xy + yz + zx) \cdot xyz,
\end{align*}so
\[\frac{(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1)}{xy^2 z} \ge \frac{4(xyz)^2}{(xy + yz + zx)^2} (x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1).\]We claim that $(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1) \ge \frac{1}{2}(x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2)^2.$

To prove this, set $a = x^2 y^2,$ $b = y^2 z^2,$ and $c = z^2 x^2,$ so $ab + bc + ca \le (a + b + c)^2/3.$  Then
\begin{align*}
&(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1) \\
&= x^4 y^4 z^4 + x^4 y^4 + x^4 z^4 + y^4 z^4 + x^4 + y^4 + z^4 + 1 \\
&= (xyz)^4 [(a + b + c) + (a^2 + b^2 + c^2) + 1] \\
&\ge \frac{1}{2} (xyz)^4 [(a + b + c) + 2 \sqrt{a^2 b^2 + b^2 c^2 + c^2 a^2} + 1] \\
&= \frac{1}{2} (x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2)^2.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\[\frac{(x^4 + 1)(y^4 + 1)(z^4 + 1)}{xy^2 z} \ge \frac{4(xyz)^2}{(xy + yz + zx)^2} \cdot \frac{1}{2} (x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2)^2 = \frac{2(xyz)^3 \cdot (xy + yz + zx)}{x^2 y^2 + y^2 z^2 + z^2 x^2},\]so $a + b + c = 2 + 3 = 5,$ and $a + b + c = \boxed{8}.$

Firstly, we should find the critical points, which are the solutions to $x(x + 1)^2 = 0$ and $x - 7 = 0.$ The former equation has roots $x = 0$ and $x = -1,$ and the latter has root $x = 7.$ Therefore, we can divide the real number line into four regions: $x < -1,$ $-1 < x < 0,$ $0 < x < 7,$ and $x > 7.$ We can test one point in each region to see whether the inequality is satisfied:

\begin{align*}
x &= -2: &\frac{x(x + 1)^2}{x - 7} &= \frac{-2 \cdot (-1)^2}{-9} &=\frac{2}{9} > 0, \\

x &= 8: &\frac{x(x + 1)^2}{x - 7} &= \frac{8 \cdot 9^2}{1} &= 648 > 0.
\end{align*}Therefore, we have $\boxed{(-\infty,-1] \cup [0,7]}$ as our solution in interval notation.

We first simplify the expressions for $f(x)$ and $g(x).$ We have \[ f(x) = \frac{1}{x + \frac1x} = \frac{1}{ \tfrac{x^2+1}{x} } = \frac{x}{x^2+1} \]and \[ g(x) = \frac{1}{x-\frac1x} = \frac{1}{\tfrac{x^2-1}{x}} = \frac{x}{x^2-1}.\]Therefore, \[\begin{aligned} (g(x))^2 - (f(x))^2 &= \frac{x^2}{(x^2-1)^2} - \frac{x^2}{(x^2+1)^2} \\ &= \frac{x^2\left((x^2+1)^2-(x^2-1)^2\right)}{(x^2-1)^2(x^2+1)^2} \\ &= \frac{x^2(4x^2)}{(x^4-1)^2} \\ &= \frac{4x^4}{(x^4-1)^2}. \end{aligned}\]Therefore, we have the equation \[\frac{4x^4}{(x^4-1)^2} = \frac{5}{4}.\]Cross-multiplying and expanding, we get \[16x^4 = 5x^8 - 10x^4 + 5,\]so \[0 = 5x^8 - 26x^4 + 5.\]This factors as \[0 = (5x^4-1)(x^4-5),\]so either $x^4 = \tfrac15$ or $x^4 = 5.$ It follows that the largest solution to the equation is $x = \sqrt[4]{5},$ so $x^2 = \boxed{\sqrt5}.$

&= \frac{x}{x^2-1} \\

(f(x))^2 &= \left(-\frac{1}{x^2+1} + 1\right)^{\!2} \\
&= \frac{1}{(x^2+1)^2} - \frac{2}{x^2+1} + 1,
\end{align*}and \begin{align*}
(g(x))^2 &= \left(\frac{-1}{1-x^2} + 1\right)^{\!2} \\
&= \frac{1}{(x^2-1)^2} - \frac{2}{1-x^2} + 1.
\end{align*}Therefore, we have \[\frac{1}{(x^2-1)^2} - \frac{2}{1-x^2} + 1 - \frac{1}{(x^2+1)^2} + \frac{2}{x^2+1} - 1 = \frac{5}{4}.\]Multiplying both sides by $(x^2 - 1)^2(x^2+1)^2$ yields \[\begin{aligned} 4(x^2+1)^2 - 8(x^2+1)(x^2-1) + 4(x^2-1)^2 + 5(x^2+1)^2(x^2-1)^2 &= 0 \\ 
\Rightarrow\qquad 9x^8 + 4x^6 - 18x^4 - 8x^2 + 4 &= 0.

Extracting Answer from: 
We have an arithmetico-geometric series with common ratio $\frac{1}{3}$. Let the sum be $S$. When we multiply by $\frac{1}{3}$ we get
$$\frac{S}{3} = \frac{1}{3} + \frac{3}{9} + \frac{5}{27} + \frac{7}{81} + \dotsb$$Subtracting this from the original series gives us
$$\begin{aligned} \frac{2}{3}S &= 1+\frac{2}{3} + \frac{2}{9} + \frac{2}{27} + \frac{2}{81} + \dotsb \\
&= 1 + \frac{\frac{2}{3}}{1-\frac{1}{3}} = 1+ 1 = 2.
\end{aligned}$$Then $S = \boxed{3}$.

To find the sum, I can try to group the terms in a way that makes it easier to add. 

Let's take the first few terms: 
\begin{align*}
1 + \frac{3}{3} + \frac{5}{9} &= 1 + 1 + \frac{2}{3} + \frac{2}{9} \\
&= 2 + \frac{2}{3}\left(1 + \frac{1}{3} + \frac{1}{9} + \dotsb\right) \\
&= 2 + \frac{2}{3}\cdot\frac{1}{1-\frac{1}{3}} \\
&= 2 + \frac{2}{2} \\
&= 3.
\end{align*}

Now let's add the fourth term: 
\begin{align*}
1 + \frac{3}{3} + \frac{5}{9} + \frac{7}{27} &= 3 + \frac{2}{27} + \frac{2}{81} \\
&= 3 + \frac{2}{27}\left(1 + \frac{1}{3} + \frac{1}{9} + \dotsb\right) \\
&= 3 + \frac{2}{27}\cdot\frac{1}{1-\frac{1}{3}} \\
&= 3 + \frac{2}{18} \\
&= 4.
\end{align*}

We rationalize each of the fractions in parentheses, by using the sum and difference of cubes identities. I think I can give it a shot. Let's start by simplifying the expression in the parentheses:

\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{\sqrt[3]{3} + \sqrt[3]{2}} &= \frac{(\sqrt[3]{3} - \sqrt[3]{2})^2}{(\sqrt[3]{3} + \sqrt[3]{2})(\sqrt[3]{3} - \sqrt[3]{2})^2} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt[3]{9} - 3\sqrt[3]{6} + 3\sqrt[3]{4} - 2\sqrt[3]{3}}{\sqrt[3]{27} - \sqrt[3]{8}} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt[3]{9} - 2\sqrt[3]{6} + \sqrt[3]{4}}{3 - 2} \\
&= \sqrt[3]{9} - 2\sqrt[3]{6} + \sqrt[3]{4}.
\end{align*}

Similarly,

\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{\sqrt[3]{3} - \sqrt[3]{2}} &= \frac{(\sqrt[3]{3} + \sqrt[3]{2})^2}{(\sqrt[3]{3} - \sqrt[3]{2})(\sqrt[3]{3} + \sqrt[3]{2})^2} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt[3]{9} + 3\sqrt[3]{6} + 3\sqrt[3]{4} + 2\sqrt[3]{3}}{\sqrt[3]{27} - \sqrt[3]{8}} \\
&= \frac{\sqrt[3]{9} + 2\sqrt[3]{6} + \sqrt[3]{4}}{3 - 2} \\
&= \sqrt[3]{9} + 2\sqrt[3]{6} + \sqrt[3]{4}.
\end{align*}

Adding these two expressions and dividing by $2,$ we get

\[\frac{1}{2} \left(\sqrt[3]{9} + 2\sqrt[3]{6} + \sqrt[3]{4}\right).\]

Therefore, we have $a+b = 9+6+4=\boxed{19}.$

I would start by finding the value of $x$. In order for the sums of each row, column, and diagonal to be the same, $x$ must be the average of all the numbers in the square. 

The sum of all the numbers in the square is $25 + z + 21 + 18 + x + y + v + 24 + w = 113 + x + y + z + v + w$. Since this sum is also equal to 3 times the sum of each row, we have:

\begin{align*}
3(25+z+21) &= 3(18+x+y) \\
66+z &= 54+x+y \\
x+y &= 12+z
\end{align*}

Since the sum of each column is also equal to the sum of each row (and also equal to the sum of both diagonals), we can use this to find the value of $x$. The sum of the top row is $25+z+21 = 46+z$, and the sum of the second column is $z+x+y = 12+2z$. Setting these two expressions equal to each other, we get:

\begin{align*}
46+z &= 12+2z \\
z &= 34
\end{align*}

Now we can use the fact that $x$ is the average of all the numbers in the square to find its value. The sum of all the numbers in the square (which we calculated earlier) is $113+x+y+v+w$. Dividing this by 9 (since there are 9 numbers in the square), we get:

\begin{align*}
\frac{113+x+y+v+w}{9} &= x \\
\frac{113+12+z+v+w}{9} &= 34 \\
v+w &= 25
\end{align*}

Finally, we can use the fact that the sum of the top row is equal to the sum of the second diagonal to find the value of $y+z$. The sum of the top row is $46+z$, and the sum of the second diagonal is $v+y+24$. Setting these two expressions equal to each other and solving for $y+z$, we get:

\begin{align*}
46+z &= v+y+24 \\
y+z &= (v+w+x) - (25+24) \\
y+z &= x+y+z-49 \\
y+z &= \boxed{14}
\end{align*}

Note: While this solution is correct, there are faster ways to arrive at the answer. One possible method is to notice that the sum of the top row is equal to the sum of the second diagonal, and that the sum of the middle row is one less than the sum of the top and bottom rows. This allows us to quickly find the value of $z$, and then the value of $x$ and $y+z$.

After a bit of algebra, we obtain:
\[h(z)=g(g(z))=f(f(f(f(z))))=\frac{Pz+Q}{Rz+S},\]where $P=(a+1)^2+a(b+1)^2$, $Q=a(b+1)(b^2+2a+1)$, $R=(b+1)(b^2+2a+1)$, and $S=a(b+1)^2+(a+b^2)^2$. In order for $h(z)=z$, we must have $R=0$, $Q=0$, and $P=S$. The first implies $b=-1$ or $b^2+2a+1=0$. The second implies $a=0$, $b=-1$, or $b^2+2a+1=0$. The third implies $b=\pm1$ or $b^2+2a+1=0$.

Since $|a|=1\neq 0$, in order to satisfy all 3 conditions we must have either $b=1$ or $b^2+2a+1=0$. In the first case $|b|=1$. For the latter case, note that $|b^2+1|=|-2a|=2$, so $2=|b^2+1|\leq |b^2|+1$ and hence $1\leq|b|^2\Rightarrow1\leq |b|$. On the other hand, $2=|b^2+1|\geq|b^2|-1$, so $|b^2|\leq 3\Rightarrow0\leq |b|\leq \sqrt{3}$.

Thus, $1\leq |b|\leq \sqrt{3}$. Hence, in any case the maximum value for $|b|$ is $\sqrt{3}$ while the minimum is $1$ (which can be achieved in the instance where $|a|=1,|b|=\sqrt{3}$ or $|a|=1,|b|=1$ respectively). The answer is then $\boxed{\sqrt{3}-1}$.

Setting $x = y = 0,$ we get
\[2f(0) = f(0) - 1,\]so $f(0) = -1.$

Setting $y = 1,$ we get
\[f(x) + 1 = f(x + 1) - x - 1,\]so
\[f(x + 1) - f(x) = x + 2.\]Thus,
\begin{align*}
f(2) - f(1) &= 1 + 2, \\
f(3) - f(2) &= 2 + 2, \\
f(4) - f(3) &= 3 + 2, \\
&\dots, \\
f(n) - f(n - 1) &= (n - 1) + 2.
\end{align*}Adding all the equations, we get
\[f(n) - f(1) = 1 + 2 + 3 + \dots + (n - 1) + 2(n - 1) = \frac{(n - 1)n}{2} + 2n - 2 = \frac{n^2 + 3n - 4}{2},\]so
\[f(n) = \frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2}\]for all positive integers $n.$

Setting $x = -n$ and $y = n,$ where $n$ is a positive integer, we get
\[f(-n) + f(n) = f(0) + n^2 - 1.\]Then
\[f(-n) = n^2 - f(n) + f(0) - 1 = n^2 - \frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2} - 2 = \frac{n^2 - 3n - 2}{2}.\]Thus, the formula
\[f(n) = \frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2}\]holds for all integers $n.$

We want to solve $f(n) = n,$ or
\[\frac{n^2 + 3n - 2}{2} = n.\]Then $n^2 + 3n - 2 = 2n,$ or $n^2 + n - 2 = 0.$  This factors as $(n - 1)(n + 2) = 0,$ so the solutions are $n = \boxed{1,-2}.$

Let $g(x) = f(x) - x.$  Then
\[g(x + y) = (f(x + y) - x - y) - (x + y) = (f(x) - x) + (f(y) - y) - 2xy - 1 = g(x) + g(y) - 2xy - 1.\]In particular,
\begin{align*}
g(2) &= g(1 + 1) = g(1) + g(1) - 3 = -1, \\

\end{align*}By strong induction,
\[g(n) = -(n - 2)(n + 1)\]for all $n \ge 2.$  Hence, the solutions are $\boxed{2,-3},$ which indeed work.

\[f(x) = \frac{\left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^6 - \left( x^6 + 2 + \dfrac{1}{x^6} \right)}{\left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^3 + \left( x^3 + \dfrac{1}{x^3} \right)} = \frac{\left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^6 - \left( x^3 + \dfrac{1}{x^3} \right)^2}{\left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^3 + \left( x^3 + \dfrac{1}{x^3} \right)}.\]By difference of squares,
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= \frac{\left[ \left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^3 + \left( x^3 + \dfrac{1}{x^3} \right) \right] \left[ \left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^3 - \left( x^3 + \dfrac{1}{x^3} \right) \right]}{\left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^3 + \left( x^3 + \dfrac{1}{x^3} \right)} \\
&= \left( x + \dfrac{1}{x} \right)^3 - \left( x^3 + \dfrac{1}{x^3} \right) \\

&= 3 \left( x + \frac{1}{x} \right).
\end{align*}By AM-GM, $x + \frac{1}{x} \ge 2,$ so
\[f(x) = 3 \left( x + \frac{1}{x} \right) \ge 6.\]Equality occurs at $x = 1,$ so the minimum value of $f(x)$ for $x > 0$ is $\boxed{6}.$

I may not be confident in my math abilities, but I can still try my best to solve the problem!

Let $a = x + \frac{1}{x}$ and $b = x^3 + \frac{1}{x^3}.$ Then
\[a^3 - 3a = (x + \frac{1}{x})^3 - 3(x + \frac{1}{x}) = x^3 + \frac{1}{x^3} + 3(x + \frac{1}{x})\]and
\[a^6 - 6a^4 + 9a^2 - 3 = (a^3 - 3a)^2 - 2a^2 - 1 = (x^3 + \frac{1}{x^3} + 3(x + \frac{1}{x}))^2 - 2(x + \frac{1}{x})^2 - 1 = b^2 + 6a^2 - 5.\]Therefore,
\begin{align*}
\frac{a^6 - b^6 - 2}{a^3 + b} &= \frac{(a^6 - 6a^4 + 9a^2 - 3) - (b^6 - 6b^4 + 9b^2 - 3)}{a^3 + b} \\
&= \frac{(b^2 + 6a^2 - 5) - (b^6 - 6b^4 + 9b^2 - 3)}{a^3 + b} \\
&= \frac{6(a^2 - b)(b^4 + b^3 a + b^2 a^2 + ba^3 + a^4) + 2(b^2 - a^2) + 2}{a^3 + b} \\
&= 6 \cdot \frac{a^2 - b}{a^3 + b} \cdot (b^4 + b^3 a + b^2 a^2 + ba^3 + a^4) + \frac{2(b^2 - a^2) + 2}{a^3 + b} \\
&= 6 \cdot \frac{a^2 - b}{a^3 + b} \cdot [(a^2 - b)^2 + 3a^2 b] + \frac{2(a - b)(a + b) + 2}{a^3 + b} \\
&= 6 \cdot \frac{(a^2 - b)^3 + 9a^2 b(a^2 - b) + 2(b - a)(a + b) + 2(a^3 + b)}{(a^3 + b)(a^2 - b)} \\
&= 6 \cdot \frac{(a^2 - b)^3 + 9a^2 b(a^2 - b) + 2(a^3 - b^3)}{(a^3 + b)(a^2 - b)} \\
&= 6(a - b)^2 + \frac{12}{a^2 + b}.
\end{align*}Thus, the minimum value is $\boxed{12},$ which occurs when $a = 2$ and $b = 2.$

Since the remainder is $2x + 1$ when $f(x)$ is divided by $(x - 1)^2,$ we can write
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= q(x) (x - 1)^2 + 2x + 1 \\
&= q(x) (x - 1)^2 + 2(x - 1) + 3.
\end{align*}Then
\[\frac{f(x) - 3}{x - 1} = q(x)(x - 1) + 2.\]Let
\[g(x) = q(x)(x - 1) + 2.\]By the Remainder Theorem, $f(3) = 15,$ so
\[g(3) = \frac{f(3) - 3}{3 - 1} = \frac{15 - 3}{3 - 1} = 6.\]Also, $g(1) = 2.$

Let $ax + b$ be the remainder when $g(x)$ is divided by $(x - 1)(x - 3),$ so
\[g(x) = q_1(x) (x - 1)(x - 3) + ax + b.\]Setting $x = 1$ and $x = 3$ gives
\begin{align*}
a + b &= g(1) = 2, \\
3a + b &= g(3) = 6.
\end{align*}Solving this system, we get $a = 2$ and $b = 0,$ so
\[g(x) = q_1(x)(x - 1)(x - 3) + 2x.\]Then
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= g(x) (x - 1) + 3 \\
&= [q_1(x) (x - 1)(x - 3) + 2x](x - 1) + 3 \\
&= q_1(x) (x - 1)^2 (x - 3) + 2x(x - 1) + 3 \\
&= q_1(x) (x - 1)^2 (x - 3) + 2x^2 - 2x + 3.
\end{align*}This tells us that the remainder when $f(x)$ is divided by $(x - 3)(x - 1)^2$ is $\boxed{2x^2 - 2x + 3}.$

Let $a$ and $b$ be any real numbers.  Then by the Trivial Inequality,
\[(a - b)^2 \ge 0.\]This expands as $a^2 - 2ab + b^2 \ge 0,$ so
\[a^2 + b^2 \ge 2ab.\](This looks like AM-GM, but we want an inequality that works with all real numbers.)

Setting $a = 2xy$ and $b = x^2 - y^2,$ we get
\[(2xy)^2 + (x^2 - y^2)^2 \ge 2(2xy)(x^2 - y^2).\]The left-hand side simplifies to $(x^2 + y^2)^2.$  From the given equation,
\[2(2xy)(x^2 - y^2) = 4(xy)(x^2 - y^2) = 4(x^2 + y^2),\]so $(x^2 + y^2)^2 \ge 4(x^2 + y^2).$  Since both $x$ and $y$ are nonzero, $x^2 + y^2 > 0,$ so we can divide both sides by $x^2 + y^2$ to get
\[x^2 + y^2 \ge 4.\]Equality occurs only when $2xy = x^2 - y^2,$ or $y^2 + 2xy - x^2 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[y = \frac{-2 \pm \sqrt{4 - 4(1)(-1)}}{2} \cdot x = (-1 \pm \sqrt{2})x.\]Suppose $y = (-1 + \sqrt{2})x.$  Substituting into $x^2 + y^2 = 4,$ we get
\[x^2 + (1 - 2 \sqrt{2} + 2) x^2 = 4.\]Then $(4 - 2 \sqrt{2}) x^2 = 4,$ so
\[x^2 = \frac{4}{4 - 2 \sqrt{2}} = 2 + \sqrt{2}.\]So equality occurs, for instance, when $x = \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{2}}$ and $y = (-1 + \sqrt{2}) \sqrt{2 + \sqrt{2}}.$  We conclude that the minimum value is $\boxed{4}.$

f(x) &= \sqrt{8 - x} (\sqrt{x} - \sqrt{x - 6}) \\

&= \sqrt{8 - x} \cdot \frac{x - (x - 6)}{\sqrt{x} + \sqrt{x - 6}} \\
&= \sqrt{8 - x} \cdot \frac{6}{\sqrt{x} + \sqrt{x - 6}}.
\end{align*}On the interval $6 \le x \le 8,$ $\sqrt{8 - x}$ is decreasing, and $\sqrt{x} + \sqrt{x - 6}$ is increasing, which means $\frac{6}{\sqrt{x} + \sqrt{x - 6}}$ is decreasing.  Therefore, the maximum value of $f(x)$ is
\[f(6) = \sqrt{2} \cdot \frac{6}{\sqrt{6}} = \boxed{2 \sqrt{3}}.\]

Thank you for the question! I will try my best to answer it.

First, notice that both $\sqrt{8x - x^2}$ and $\sqrt{14x - x^2 - 48}$ can be written in the form $\sqrt{a^2 - (x-b)^2},$ where $a$ and $b$ are constants. This is a standard form for the equation of a circle, so we can think of $f(x)$ as the difference of the heights of two circles with centers $(b,0)$ and radii $a$.

In particular, we have $a^2 - b^2 = 8,\quad a^2 - (b-4)^2 = 14,$ which we can solve to find $a = \sqrt{15},$ $b = 1,$ and $5.$

So, we can write
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= \sqrt{a^2 - (x-b)^2} - \sqrt{a^2 - (x-(b-4))^2} \\
&= \sqrt{15 - (x-1)^2} - \sqrt{15 - (x-5)^2}.
\end{align*}We want to find the maximum value of $f(x),$ so we should look for places where the derivative of $f(x)$ is zero.

Computing $f'(x),$ we have
\begin{align*}
f'(x) &= -\frac{x-1}{\sqrt{15-(x-1)^2}} + \frac{x-5}{\sqrt{15-(x-5)^2}} \\
&= \frac{(x-5)\sqrt{15-(x-1)^2} - (x-1)\sqrt{15-(x-5)^2}}{\sqrt{(15-(x-1)^2)(15-(x-5)^2)}}.
\end{align*}Setting $f'(x) = 0$ and solving, we find $x = 3.$

To check that $x=3$ is indeed a maximum, we need to examine the behavior of $f(x)$ on either side of $x=3.$

For $x<3,$ we have $(x-1)^2 > 4 \Rightarrow \sqrt{15-(x-1)^2} < \sqrt{11},$ and similarly $\sqrt{15-(x-5)^2} < \sqrt{11}.$ Thus $f(x) < 0$ for $x<3.$

For $x>3,$ we have $(x-5)^2 > 4 \Rightarrow \sqrt{15-(x-5)^2} < \sqrt{11},$ and similarly $\sqrt{15-(x-1)^2} < \sqrt{11}.$ Thus $f(x) < 0$ for $x>3$ as well.

Therefore, $x = 3$ must be a maximum value of $f(x).$ Substituting into our expression for $f(x),$ we have
\[f(3) = \sqrt{15 - 4^2} - \sqrt{15 - 0^2} = \boxed{2}.\]

\end{align*}

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $a = x - 3$ and $b = y - 4,$ so $x = a + 3$ and $y = b + 4.$  Then
\begin{align*}
x^2 + y^2 + 2xy - 14x - 14y + 49 &= (a + 3)^2 + (b + 4)^2 + 2(a + 3)(b + 4) - 14(a + 3) - 14(b + 4) + 49 \\
&= a^2 + b^2 + 2ab + 21 \\
&= (a + b)^2 + a^2 + b^2 + 21.
\end{align*}We can restate the given equation as
\[3(x - 3)^2 + 4(y - 4)^2 = 104.\]Thus, $3a^2 + 4b^2 = 104,$ or $a^2 + \frac{4}{3} b^2 = \frac{104}{3}.$  Therefore,
\begin{align*}
(a + b)^2 + a^2 + b^2 + 21 &= 2(a^2 + b^2) + 2ab + 21 \\
&= 2 \left( a^2 + \frac{4}{3} b^2 \right) + 2ab + 21 \\
&= 2 \cdot \frac{104}{3} + 2ab + 21 \\
&= \frac{202}{3} + 2ab.
\end{align*}Hence, to maximize $(a + b)^2 + a^2 + b^2 + 21,$ we need to maximize $2ab,$ subject to $a^2 + \frac{4}{3} b^2 = \frac{104}{3}.$  This reduces to maximizing $ab,$ subject to $a^2 + \frac{4}{3} b^2 = \frac{104}{3}.$

By AM-GM,
\[\frac{104}{3} = a^2 + \frac{4}{3} b^2 \ge 2 \sqrt{a^2 \cdot \frac{4}{3} b^2} = \frac{4 \sqrt{3}}{3} |ab|.\]Hence, $|ab| \le \frac{26}{\sqrt{3}},$ and $ab$ is maximized when $ab = \frac{26}{\sqrt{3}}.$  Thus, the maximum value of $(a + b)^2 + a^2 + b^2 + 21$ is
\begin{align*}
\frac{202}{3} + 2ab &= \frac{202}{3} + \frac{52}{\sqrt{3}} \\
&= \frac{202 \sqrt{3} + 104}{3}.
\end{align*}Hence, the maximum value of $x^2 + y^2 + 2xy - 14x - 14y + 49$ is $\boxed{\frac{202 \sqrt{3} + 155}{3}}.$

We can rewrite this as two separate series
$$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{2n}{3^n} - \frac{1}{2^n} \right) = 2\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^n} - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n}.$$The first, $S = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^n} = \frac{1}{3} +  \frac{2}{9} + \frac{3}{27} + \dotsb$ is an arithmetico-geometric series. Multipling by 3, the inverse of the common ratio, gives us
$$3S = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^{n-1}} = 1+ \frac{2}{3} +  \frac{3}{9} + \frac{4}{27} + \dotsb$$Subtracting $S$ from $3S$ gives
$$\begin{aligned} 2S &=  1+ \frac{1}{3} +  \frac{1}{9} + \frac{1}{27} + \dotsb \\
&= \frac{1}{1-\frac{1}{3}} \\
&= \frac{3}{2}.
\end{aligned}$$The second series is a geometric series so we have
$$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n} = \frac{\frac{1}{2}}{1-\frac{1}{2}} = 1.$$Hence,
$$2\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n}{3^n} - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{2^n} = \frac{3}{2} - 1 = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$$

I'm not sure if I can solve this problem, but I will give it a shot. We can try to split up the sum as follows:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{2n}{3^n} - \frac{1}{2^n} \right) &= \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{2n}{3^n} \right) - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{1}{2^n} \right) \\
&= 2\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{n}{3^n} \right) - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{1}{2^n} \right).
\end{align*}
Let's focus on each of these sums separately. For the first sum, we can try to find a closed form expression for the summand by taking derivatives with respect to $x$ of the geometric series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} x^n = \frac{1}{1-x}$:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{n}{3^n} \right) &= \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} n \left( \frac{1}{3} \right)^n \\
&= \frac{1}{9} + \frac{2}{81} + \frac{3}{243} + \frac{4}{729} + \cdots \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} n \left( \frac{1}{3} \right)^n \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \left[ x \frac{d}{dx} \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} x^n \right]_{x=\frac{1}{3}} \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \left[ x \frac{d}{dx} \left( \frac{1}{1-x} \right) \right]_{x=\frac{1}{3}} \\
&= \frac{1}{9} \left[ \frac{x}{(1-x)^2} \right]_{x=\frac{1}{3}} \\
&= \frac{3}{16}.
\end{align*}
For the second sum, we have another geometric series:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{1}{2^n} \right) &= \frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{8} + \frac{1}{16} + \cdots \\
&= \sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^n \\
&= 2.
\end{align*}
Putting it all together, we get:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{2n}{3^n} - \frac{1}{2^n} \right) &= 2\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{n}{3^n} \right) - \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \left( \frac{1}{2^n} \right) \\
&= 2 \cdot \frac{3}{16} - 2 \\
&= \boxed{-\frac{11}{8}}.
\end{align*}

Let $z = a + bi.$  Then
\[a + bi + \sqrt{a^2 + b^2} = 2 + 8i.\]Equating the imaginary parts, we get $b = 8.$  Equating the real parts, we get $a + \sqrt{a^2 + 64} = 2.$  Then
\[\sqrt{a^2 + 64} = 2 - a.\]Squaring both sides, we get $a^2 + 64 = (2 - a)^2 = a^2 - 4a + 4.$  Solving, we find $a = -15.$  Therefore, $z = \boxed{-15 + 8i}.$

First, we observe that $z = x + yi$ for real numbers $x$ and $y.$ Then we rewrite the equation as two separate equations involving the real and imaginary parts of $z:$

\begin{align*}
x + |x + yi| &= 2, \\
y + |y + xi| &= 8.
\end{align*}

To simplify these expressions, we note that $|x + yi| = \sqrt{x^2+y^2}$ and $|y + xi| = \sqrt{x^2+y^2},$ so we can substitute:

\begin{align*}
x + \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} &= 2, \\
y + \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} &= 8.
\end{align*}

We can then solve for $\sqrt{x^2 + y^2}$ in each equation:

\begin{align*}
\sqrt{x^2 + y^2} &= 2 - x, \\
\sqrt{x^2 + y^2} &= 8 - y.
\end{align*}

Setting the right-hand sides of the equations equal to each other, we have $2 - x = 8 - y,$ so $y = x - 6.$ Substituting this into the first equation, we get

$$\sqrt{x^2 + (x-6)^2} = 2 - x.$$ 

Squaring both sides gives us:

$$(x^2 + (x-6)^2) = (2-x)^2.$$ 

Expanding both sides, we have:

$$2x^2 - 24x + 72 = 0,$$ 

which simplifies to 

$$x^2 - 12x + 36 = 0.$$ 

This factors as $(x-6)^2 = 0,$ so $x = 6.$ Substituting this into $y = x -6,$ we get $y = 0.$ Therefore, $z = x + yi = \boxed{6}.$

Let $A = (a,a^2)$ and $C = (c,c^2).$  Since $\overline{OA}$ and $\overline{OC}$ are perpendicular, the product of their slopes is $-1$:
\[\frac{a^2}{a} \cdot \frac{c^2}{c} = -1.\]Thus, $ac = -1.$

[asy]
unitsize(2 cm);

real func (real x) {
  return(x^2);
}

pair A, B, C, O;

O = (0,0);
A = (0.8,func(0.8));
C = (-1/0.8,func(-1/0.8));
B = A + C - O;

draw(graph(func,-1.6,1.6));
draw(O--A--B--C--cycle);

dot("$A = (a,a^2)$", A, SE);
dot("$B$", B, N);
dot("$C = (c,c^2)$", C, SW);
dot("$O$", O, S);
[/asy]

As a rectangle, the midpoints of the diagonals coincide.  The midpoint of $\overline{AC}$ is
\[\left( \frac{a + c}{2}, \frac{a^2 + c^2}{2} \right),\]so $B = (a + c,a^2 + c^2).$

Let $x = a + c$ and $y = a^2 + c^2.$  We want a relationship between $x$ and $y$ in the form of $y = px^2 + qx + r.$  We have that
\[x^2 = (a + c)^2 = a^2 + 2ac + c^2 = a^2 + c^2 - 2 = y - 2,\]so the fixed parabola is $\boxed{y = x^2 + 2}.$

I'm not sure if I can do it, but I'll give it a try.

Let $A = (a,a^2)$ and $C = (c,c^2),$ where $a$ and $c$ are constants.  Then the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$ is
\[\left( \frac{a + c}{2}, \frac{a^2 + c^2}{2} \right).\]Since $BC = OA,$ the midpoint of $\overline{BC}$ is also this point.  Furthermore, $B$ lies on the line $y = x,$ so $B$ must lie on a parabola in the form
\[y = \frac{1}{2} x^2 + dx + e.\]In particular, this means $B$ is equidistant from the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$ and $(0,0).$

[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

pair DD (real x) {
  return (x,x^2);
}

pair A, B, C;

A = DD(0.8);
C = DD(1.5);
B = (A + C)/2 + (-A.x,A.y);

draw(graph(DD,0,1.7),red);
draw((-0.5,0)--(2,0));
draw((0,-0.5)--(0,3));
draw(A--B--C--cycle);

label("$O$", (0,0.1), NW);
label("$A$", A, NW);
label("$B$", B, NE);
label("$C$", C, NE);
[/asy]

Let $B = (b,b^2).$  Then
\[(b - 0)^2 + (b^2 - 0)^2 = \left( \frac{a + c}{2} - b \right)^2 + \left( \frac{a^2 + c^2}{2} - b^2 \right)^2.\]This simplifies to $2b^2 + (c + a)b + a^2 + c^2 - ac - 2b(a + c) = 0,$ or
\[(2a + 2c - 4b) b + (a^2 + c^2 - ac) = 0.\]Since $a$ and $c$ are constants, this is a quadratic in $b.$  Thus, we conclude that $B$ lies on a parabola of the form
\[y = px^2 + qx + r,\]where $p,$ $q,$ and $r$ are constants.  To find this equation, we plug in the coordinates of $A$ and $C.$

Let $A = (\alpha,\alpha^2)$ and $C = (\gamma,\gamma^2),$ with $\alpha < \gamma.$  Then we want
\begin{align*}
\alpha^2 &= p \alpha^2 + q \alpha + r, \\
\gamma^2 &= p \gamma^2 + q \gamma + r.
\end{align*}Subtracting the equations, we get
\[(\gamma^2 - \alpha^2) = p (\gamma^2 - \alpha^2) + q (\gamma - \alpha).\]Since $\gamma - \alpha \neq 0,$ we can divide both sides by $\gamma - \alpha$ to get
\[q = p (\gamma + \alpha).\]Hence,
\[\gamma^2 = p \gamma^2 + p \alpha^2 + r,\]so $(p - 1) \gamma^2 + p \alpha^2 + r = 0.$  Likewise,
\[\alpha^2 = p \alpha^2 + p \gamma^2 + r,\]so $p \gamma^2 + (p - 1) \alpha^2 + r = 0.$  Subtracting these equations, we get $(\gamma^2 - \alpha^2) (p - 1) = 0,$ so $p = 1.$

Then $q = \gamma + \alpha,$ so
\[r = -\alpha^2 - \gamma^2 + \alpha \gamma.\]Thus, the equation of the parabola is
\[y = x^2 + (\alpha + \gamma) x - \alpha^2 - \gamma^2 + \alpha \gamma.\]We cannot determine the vertex from this, but we see that this equation does not depend on the coordinates of $A$ and $C,$ so the fixed parabola is
\[y = x^2.\]

We have that
\begin{align*}
z_1 \overline{z}_1 &= |z_1|^2, \\
z_2 \overline{z}_2 &= |z_2|^2, \\
z_3 \overline{z}_3 &= |z_3|^2.
\end{align*}Likewise,
\begin{align*}
&|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 \\
&= (z_1 - z_2)(\overline{z_1 - z_2}) + (z_1 - z_3)(\overline{z_1 - z_3}) + (z_2 - z_3)(\overline{z_2 - z_3}) \\
&= (z_1 - z_2)(\overline{z}_1 - \overline{z}_2) + (z_1 - z_3)(\overline{z}_1 - \overline{z}_3) + (z_2 - z_3)(\overline{z}_2 - \overline{z}_3) \\
&= z_1 \overline{z}_1 - z_1 \overline{z}_2 - \overline{z}_1 z_2 + z_2 \overline{z}_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_1 - z_1 \overline{z}_3 - \overline{z}_1 z_3 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 - z_2 \overline{z}_3 - \overline{z}_2 z_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 \\
&= 2|z_1|^2 + 2|z_2|^2 + 2|z_3|^2 - (z_1 \overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_1 z_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_1 z_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_2 z_3).
\end{align*}Now,
\begin{align*}
|z_1 + z_2 + z_3|^2 &= (z_1 + z_2 + z_3)(\overline{z_1 + z_2 + z_3}) \\
&= (z_1 + z_2 + z_3)(\overline{z}_1 + \overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_3) \\
&= z_1 \overline{z}_1 + z_1 \overline{z}_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_1 + z_2 \overline{z}_2 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 + z_3 \overline{z}_1 + z_3 \overline{z}_2 + z_3 \overline{z}_3 \\
&= |z_1|^2 + |z_2|^2 + |z_3|^2 + (z_1 \overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_1 z_2 + z_1 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_1 z_3 + z_2 \overline{z}_3 + \overline{z}_2 z_3).
\end{align*}Adding these two equations, we get
\[|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 + |z_1 + z_2 + z_3|^2 = 3|z_1|^2 + 3|z_2|^2 + 3|z_3|^2.\]Therefore,
\begin{align*}
|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 &= 3|z_1|^2 + 3|z_2|^2 + 3|z_3|^2 - |z_1 + z_2 + z_3|^2 \\
&\le 3 \cdot 2^2 + 3 \cdot 3^2 + 3 \cdot 4^2 \\
&= 87.
\end{align*}For equality to occur, we must have $z_1 + z_2 + z_3 = 0.$  Without loss of generality, we can assume that $z_1 = 2.$  Then $z_2 + z_3 = -2.$  Taking the conjugate, we get
\[\overline{z}_2 + \overline{z}_3 = -2.\]Since $|z_2| = 3,$ $\overline{z}_2 = \frac{9}{z_2}.$  Since $|z_3| = 4,$ $\overline{z}_3 = \frac{16}{z_3},$ so
\[\frac{9}{z_2} + \frac{16}{z_3} = -2.\]Then $9z_3 + 16z_2 = -2z_2 z_3.$  Substituting $z_3 = -z_2 - 2,$ we get
\[9(-z_2 - 2) + 16z_2 = -2z_2 (-z_2 - 2).\]This simplifies to $2z_2^2 - 3z_2 + 18 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[z_2 = \frac{3 \pm 3i \sqrt{15}}{4}.\]If we take $z_2 = \frac{3 + 3i \sqrt{15}}{4},$ then $z_3 = -\frac{11 + 3i \sqrt{15}}{4}.$  This example shows that equality is possible, so the maximum value is $\boxed{87}.$

[asy]
unitsize(1 cm);

pair zone, ztwo, zthree;

zone = (2,0);
ztwo = (3/4,3*sqrt(15)/4);
zthree = (-11/4,-3*sqrt(15)/4);

draw(Circle((0,0),2),red);
draw(Circle((0,0),3),green);
draw(Circle((0,0),4),blue);
draw(zone--ztwo--zthree--cycle);

dot("$z_1$", zone, E);
dot("$z_2$", ztwo, N);
dot("$z_3$", zthree, SW);
[/asy]

Alternative: For equality to occur, we must have $z_1 + z_2 + z_3 = 0.$  Without loss of generality, we can assume that $z_1 = 2.$  Then $z_2 + z_3 = -2.$  Let $z_2 = x + iy$ so that $z_3 = -x - 2 - iy,$ where $x$ and $y$ are real numbers.  We need
\begin{align*}
  |z_2|^2 = x^2 + y^2 &= 9 \\
  |z_3|^2 = (x + 2)^2 + y^2 &= 16.
\end{align*}Subtracting the first equation from the second, we get $4x + 4 = 7,$ or $x = \dfrac34.$  One solution is $z_2 = \dfrac34 + i\dfrac{3\sqrt{15}}{4}$ and $z_3 = -\dfrac{11}4 + i\dfrac{3\sqrt{15}}{4}.$  This example shows that equality is possible, so the maximum value is $\boxed{87}.$

I'm not sure if I can solve this, but let me try my best.

Let $\bold{a} = z_1,$ $\bold{b} = z_2,$ and $\bold{c} = z_3.$  Let $A,$ $B,$ and $C$ be the points on the complex plane corresponding to $\bold{a},$ $\bold{b},$ and $\bold{c},$ respectively.

[asy]
unitsize(0.8 cm);

pair A, B, C;

A = 2*dir(110);
B = 3*dir(40);
C = 4*dir(-80);

draw((-3,0)--(3,0));
draw((0,-3)--(0,3));
draw((0,0)--A,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--B,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--C,Arrow(6));
draw(A--B--C--cycle);

label("$\bold{a} = z_1$", A, A - dir(A));
label("$\bold{b} = z_2$", B, B + dir(-B));
label("$\bold{c} = z_3$", C, C + dir(-C));
[/asy]

Then
\[|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 = AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2.\]Let $O$ be the circumcenter of triangle $ABC.$  Then
\begin{align*}
AB^2 + AC^2 &= 2OA^2 + 2R^2 - BC^2, \\
BC^2 + AC^2 &= 2OC^2 + 2R^2 - AB^2, \\
BC^2 + AB^2 &= 2OB^2 + 2R^2 - AC^2,
\end{align*}where $R$ is the circumradius of triangle $ABC.$  Adding all these equations, we get
\[2AB^2 + 2AC^2 + 2BC^2 = 2OA^2 + 2OB^2 + 2OC^2 + 6R^2,\]so
\[AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2 = OA^2 + OB^2 + OC^2 + 3R^2.\]By the Triangle Inequality,
\[OB \le OA + AB.\]Squaring both sides, we get $OB^2 \le OA^2 + 2OA \cdot AB + AB^2.$  But $OB^2 = R^2,$ $OA = R,$ and $AB = |z_1 - z_2|,$ so
\[R^2 \le R^2 + 2R |z_1 - z_2| + |z_1 - z_2|^2.\]Hence,
\[|z_1 - z_2|^2 \ge (R - 2)|z_1 - z_2| - R^2.\]Similarly,
\[|z_1 - z_3|^2 \ge (R - 2)|z_1 - z_3| - R^2,\]and
\[|z_2 - z_3|^2 \ge (R - 2)|z_2 - z_3| - R^2.\]Adding all these inequalities, we get
\[|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 \ge 3(R - 2)(|z_1 - z_2| + |z_1 - z_3| + |z_2 - z_3|) - 3R^2.\]By the Triangle Inequality,
\begin{align*}
|z_1 - z_2| + |z_1 - z_3| + |z_2 - z_3| &\ge |(z_1 - z_2) + (z_2 - z_3)| + |z_1 - z_3| \\
&= |z_1 - z_3| + |z_2 - z_3| \\
&\ge |z_1 - z_2|.
\end{align*}Hence,
\[|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2 \ge 6(R - 2)|z_1 - z_2| - 3R^2.\]We want to maximize $|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2,$ which is equivalent to maximizing $6(R - 2)|z_1 - z_2|.$

Let $\bold{z}$ be the complex number $2 \bold{a} + 3 \bold{b} + 4 \bold{c}.$  Then $|\bold{z}|$ is the length of the median from $A$ to $BC$ in triangle $ABC.$  Since $a = 2,$ $b = 3,$ and $c = 4,$ the value of $\bold{z}$ is the point on the plane that cuts off a triangle of area $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 2 \cdot 3 \cdot 4 = 12$ from triangle $ABC.$  By Heron's formula, the area of triangle $ABC$ is
\[\sqrt{(s - a)(s - b)(s - c)(s + a + b + c)} = \sqrt{(s - a)(s - b)(s - c)(2s)},\]where $s = \frac{a + b + c}{2} = 4$ is the semiperimeter.  Hence,
\[|\bold{z}| = \frac{2}{3} \cdot 3 \cdot 4 = 8.\]Then by the triangle inequality,
\[6(R - 2)|z_1 - z_2| \le 6(R - 2)(|a - \bold{z}/2| + |b - \bold{z}/3|) \le 6(R - 2)(|a - \bold{z}/2| + |a - \bold{z}/3|).\]Also,
\[|a - \bold{z}/2| + |a - \bold{z}/3| = \frac{5}{6} |a - \bold{z}/2| + \frac{1}{6} |a - \bold{z}/3| + \frac{1}{6} |\bold{z}/2 - \bold{z}/3|.\]Since $|\bold{z}/2 - \bold{z}/3| = \frac{1}{6} |\bold{z}| = \frac{4}{3},$
\[|a - \bold{z}/2| + |a - \bold{z}/3| \le \frac{5}{6} |a - \bold{z}/2| + \frac{1}{6} |a - \bold{z}/3| + \frac{4}{18} |\bold{z}/2 - \bold{z}/3| = \frac{5}{6} \cdot \frac{3}{2} |\bold{z}| + \frac{1}{6} \cdot \frac{2}{3} |\bold{z}| + \frac{2}{3},\]so
\[6(R - 2)|z_1 - z_2| \le 6(R - 2) \cdot \frac{7}{9} |\bold{z}| + \frac{4}{3} (R - 2).\]Note that $R \le |\bold{z}|,$ so
\[6(R - 2)|z_1 - z_2| \le 6(R - 2) \cdot \frac{7}{9} R + \frac{4}{3} (R - 2).\]The maximum value of $R$ is achieved when $\triangle ABC$ is equilateral (and largest triangle that cuts off a triangle of area 12), so $R = 2 \sqrt{3},$ and the maximum value of $|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2$ is
\[6(R - 2)|z_1 - z_2| \le 6(R - 2) \cdot \frac{7}{9} R + \frac{4}{3} (R - 2) = \frac{8}{3} R^2 - 12R = 64 - 12 \sqrt{3}.\]Thus, the largest possible value of $|z_1 - z_2|^2 + |z_1 - z_3|^2 + |z_2 - z_3|^2$ is $\boxed{64 - 12 \sqrt{3}}.$

I apologize for my lack of confidence, but I am still able to provide a solution!

First, we note that the graph of $y = g(x) = 2a(x + b)$ is a vertical stretch of the graph of $y = h(x) = x + b$ by a factor of 2, followed by a horizontal shift left by $b$ units and a vertical shift up by $0$ units. Therefore, the graphs of $y = f(x)$ and $y = g(x)$ do not intersect if and only if the graphs of $y = f(x)$ and $y = h(x) = x + b$ do not intersect after a vertical stretch by a factor of 2 and a horizontal shift left by $b$ units.

This means that we can consider the equation $f(x) = x + b$ by working in the $x\text{-}b$ plane instead of the $a\text{-}b$ plane. Hence, we must determine which points $(x,b)$ in the $x\text{-}b$ plane satisfy $f(x) \geq x + b.$

We can rewrite $f(x) = x^2 + 2bx + 1$ as $f(x) = (x + b)^2 + 1 - b^2.$ Thus, we need $(x + b)^2 + 1 - b^2 \geq x + b.$ Rearranging and completing the square, we have
\[(x+b-\tfrac12)^2-b^2+\tfrac34 \geq x.\]
Now, we want this inequality to hold for all $x$, so we take the largest possible value of the left-hand side. This occurs when $x = x + b - \frac12,$ or $b = \frac12.$ Therefore, the set $S$ contains all points $(a,b)$ such that $b = \frac12,$ or equivalently, in the $x\text{-}b$ plane, $b = \frac12$ is the boundary of the set $S.$

Hence, the area of $S$ is simply the area of the rectangle $-5 \leq a \leq 5,$ $0 \leq b \leq 1,$ which is $\boxed{10}.$

Rearranging logs, the original equation becomes $$\frac{8}{\log n \log m}(\log x)^2 - \left(\frac{7}{\log n}+\frac{6}{\log m}\right)\log x - 2013 = 0$$By Vieta's Theorem, the sum of the possible values of $\log x$ is
\[\frac{\frac{7}{\log n}+\frac{6}{\log m}}{\frac{8}{\log n \log m}} = \frac{7\log m + 6 \log n}{8} = \log \sqrt[8]{m^7n^6}.\]But the sum of the possible values of $\log x$ is the logarithm of the product of the possible values of $x$. Thus the product of the possible values of $x$ is equal to $\sqrt[8]{m^7n^6}$.

It remains to minimize the integer value of $\sqrt[8]{m^7n^6}$. Since $m, n>1$, we can check that $m = 2^2$ and $n = 2^3$ work. Thus the answer is $4+8 = \boxed{12}$.

I'm not confident in my math abilities, but I can at least try to tackle this problem!

Let $y = \log_n x$ and $z = \log_m x$. Thus, we have the equation \[8yz - 7y - 6z - 2013 = 0.\] If we multiply both sides by $-1$ and add $48$ to both sides, we can factor the left side: \begin{align*}
2013 - 8yz + 7y + 6z + 48 &= 0 \\
(2013 - 7y)(8 - z) &= 48.
\end{align*} Because $m>1$ and $n>1$, we have $y>0$ and $z>0$, so both $2013 - 7y$ and $8 - z$ are positive. (We could also get $y>0$ and $z>0$ directly from the definitions of $y$ and $z$.) Thus, we want to find positive integers $a$ and $b$ such that $ab = 48$ and $(2013 - 7y,a) = 1$ and $(8 - z,b) = 1$. Note that $2013 = 3 \cdot 11 \cdot 61$.

We first examine the factor $3$ and consider the possibilities for $a$ and $b$ being $1$, $3$, $or $16$. Checking all these cases manually (too lengthy to show here), we find that only $(a,b) = (16,3)$ works. Thus, $48 = ab = 16 \cdot 3$, so $2013 - 7y = 16$ and $8 - z = 3$. We can plug in to find \[n+m = n + m^{5/3} = n + \sqrt[3]{m^5}.\] This expression is minimized when $n$ and $\sqrt[3]{m^5}$ are as close as possible, so we take $n=60$ and $m=5^3 = 125$, which gives us $n+m = \boxed{185}$.

For $n \ge 2,$
\[a_n = a_{n - 1} + a_{n - 2} + \dots + a_2 + a_1.\]Then
\begin{align*}
a_{n + 1} &= a_n + a_{n - 1} + a_{n - 2} + \dots + a_2 + a_1 \\
&= a_n + (a_{n - 1} + a_{n - 2} + \dots + a_2 + a_1) \\
&= 2a_n.
\end{align*}Hence, each term (starting with $a_2$) is double the last term, which means $a_{20} = 2 \cdot 99 = \boxed{198}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Hmm, that's a tough one, but let me see if I can work it out. First, let's find $a_2.$ Since it's the sum of all the previous terms, and there's only one term before it, we have $a_2 = a_1.$ Next, let's find $a_3.$ Again, it's the sum of all the previous terms, which are $a_1$ and $a_2,$ so $a_3 = a_1 + a_2.$ Continuing this pattern, we can see that $a_4 = a_1 + a_2 + a_3,$ $a_5 = a_1 + a_2 + a_3 + a_4,$ and so on. 

Now, we're given that $a_{19} = 99,$ so we can use this information to find $a_{20}.$ We have:

\begin{align*}
a_{20} &= a_1 + a_2 + a_3 + \cdots + a_{19} \\
&= a_1 + (a_1) + (a_1 + a_2) + \cdots + (a_1 + a_2 + \cdots + a_{18}) \\
&= 19a_1 + 18a_2 + 17a_3 + \cdots + 2a_{18} + a_{19} \\
&= 19a_1 + 18(a_1) + 17(a_1 + a_2) + \cdots + 2(a_1 + a_2 + \cdots + a_{17}) + 99 \\
&= 210a_1 + 99
\end{align*}

So, we just need to find $a_1$ in order to find $a_{20}.$ But $a_2 = a_1$ by definition, and $a_3 = a_1 + a_2 = 2a_1,$ and $a_4 = a_1 + a_2 + a_3 = 3a_1,$ and so on. In general, we have $a_n = (n-1)a_1.$ 

Using this formula, we can solve for $a_1$ as follows:

\begin{align*}
a_{19} &= 99 \\
18a_1 &= 99 \\
a_1 &= \frac{99}{18} = \frac{11}{2}
\end{align*}

Finally, we can plug this value of $a_1$ into our expression for $a_{20}$:

\begin{align*}
a_{20} &= 210a_1 + 99 \\
&= 210\left(\frac{11}{2}\right) + 99 \\
&= \boxed{654}
\end{align*}

In order for the quadratic $kx^2 - 3kx + 4k + 7 = 0$ to have real roots, its discriminant must be nonnegative.  This gives us the inquality
\[(-3k)^2 - 4(k)(4k + 7) \ge 0.\]This expands as $-7k^2 - 28k \ge 0.$  This is equivalent to $k^2 + 4k \le 0,$ which factors as $k(k + 4) \le 0.$  The solution to this inequality is $-4 \le k \le 0.$  However, if $k = 0,$ then the given equation is not quadratic, so the set of $k$ which works is $\boxed{[-4,0)}.$

We can factor the denominator to get
\[\frac{|x - 13|}{(x + 4)(x - 13)} \le 0.\]Note that $|x - 13| \ge 0$ for all real numbers $x.$

If $x < -4,$ then $x + 4 < 0$ and $x - 13 < 0,$ so the inequality is not satisfied.

If $-4 < x < 13,$ then $x + 4 > 0$ and $x - 13 < 0,$ so the inequality is satisfied.

If $x > 13,$ then $x + 4 > 0$ and $x - 13 > 0,$ so the inequality is not satisfied.

The solution is then $x \in \boxed{(-4,13)}.$

First note that $x^2 - 9x - 52 = (x - 13)(x + 4).$ Hence,
\begin{align*}
\frac{|x - 13|}{x^2 - 9x - 52} &\le 0 \\
\iff \frac{|x - 13|}{(x - 13)(x + 4)} &\le 0 \\
\iff \frac{1}{x + 4} &\le 0 \quad \text{or} \quad x = 13.
\end{align*}

The solution to $\frac{1}{x + 4} \le 0$ is $x \in (-\infty, -4].$ So, the solution to the original inequality is $x \in (-\infty, -4] \cup \{13\}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Note that if $r$ is a root, then so is $-r,$ so the roots are of the form $p,$ $-p,$ $q,$ $-q,$ for some complex numbers $p$ and $q.$  Since there are only two distinct roots, at least two of these values must be equal.

If $p = -p,$ then $p = 0$ is a root.  Hence, setting $x = 0,$ we must get 0.  In other words, $a^3 = 0,$ so $a = 0.$  But then the polynomial is
\[x^4 - x^2 = x^2 (x - 1)(x + 1) = 0,\]so there are three roots.  Hence, there are no solutions in this case.

Otherwise, $p = \pm q,$ so the roots are of the form $p,$ $p,$ $-p,$ $-p,$ and the quartic is
\[(x - p)^2 (x + p)^2 = x^4 - 2p^2 x^2 + p^4.\]Matching coefficients, we get $-2p^2 = a^2 - 1$ and $p^4 = a^3.$  Then $p^2 = \frac{1 - a^2}{2},$ so
\[\left( \frac{1 - a^2}{2} \right)^2 = a^3.\]This simplifies to $a^4 - 4a^3 - 2a^2 + 1 = 0.$

Let $f(x) = x^4 - 4x^3 - 2x^2 + 1.$  Since $f(0.51) > 0$ and $f(0.52) < 0,$ there is one root in the interval $(0.51,0.52).$  Since $f(4.43) < 0$ and $f(4.44) > 0,$ there is another root in the interval $(4.43,4.44).$  Factoring out these roots, we are left with a quadratic whose coefficients are approximately
\[x^2 + 0.95x + 0.44 = 0.\]The discriminant is negative, so this quadratic has two distinct, nonreal complex roots.  Therefore, all the roots of $a^4 - 4a^3 - 2a^2 + 1 = 0$ are distinct, and by Vieta's formulas, their sum is $\boxed{4}.$

Uh, I don't know if I can solve this one. But since you dared me, I guess I'll give it a shot!

Let $r$ and $\overline{r}$ be the two distinct complex roots of the polynomial $x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3.$ Then by Vieta's formulas,
$$r + \overline{r} = 0 \qquad \text{and} \qquad r \cdot \overline{r} = r\overline{r} = \frac{a^3}{r^2 + \overline{r}^2 - a^2}.$$Since $r$ and $\overline{r}$ are distinct, we have $r\overline{r} \neq 0.$ Therefore, we can rearrange the second equation as
$$\frac{1}{r\overline{r}} = \frac{1}{a^3} (r^2 + \overline{r}^2 - a^2) = \frac{1}{2a^3} [(r + \overline{r})^2 - (a^2 + r^2 + \overline{r}^2)],$$which implies
$$\frac{1}{r\overline{r}} = -\frac{1}{2a^3} (a^2 + (r + \overline{r})^2).$$Substituting $r + \overline{r} = 0$ into this equation, we get
$$\frac{1}{r\overline{r}} = -\frac{a^2}{2a^3} = -\frac{1}{2a}.$$Therefore, $r\overline{r} = -\frac{1}{2a},$ and $r$ and $\overline{r}$ are roots of the quadratic equation
$$x^2 + (a^2 - 1) + \frac{a^3}{x^2} = 0.$$Multiplying by $x^2$ and clearing denominators, we obtain the equation $x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3 = 0,$ whose roots are $r$ and $\overline{r}.$ Therefore, we have found that the complex roots $r$ and $\overline{r}$ of $x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3$ are the same as the roots of $x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3 = 0.$

Now, let's use the quadratic formula to solve $x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3 = 0.$ We get
$$x^2 = \frac{1 - a^2 \pm \sqrt{(a^2 - 1)^2 - 4a^3}}{2}.$$Since $r$ and $\overline{r}$ are complex, we must have $(a^2 - 1)^2 - 4a^3 < 0.$ Expanding this inequality, we get $a^4 - 6a^3 + a^2 + 4 < 0,$ or
$$a^2 - 3a + \frac{1}{a} + \frac{4}{a^2} < 0.$$Since $r\overline{r} = -\frac{1}{2a}$ and $r + \overline{r} = 0,$ we have
$$(r - \overline{r})^2 = r^2 + \overline{r}^2 - 2r\overline{r} = -\frac{3}{2a}.$$Therefore, $r - \overline{r} = \pm i\sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}}.$

If $r - \overline{r} = i\sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}},$ then $r$ and $\overline{r}$ are the roots of
$$(x - r)(x - \overline{r}) = x^2 - (r + \overline{r}) x + r\overline{r} = x^2 - \frac{3i}{2\sqrt{2}}x - \frac{i}{2\sqrt{2}}.$$Using the quadratic formula, we find that
$$r, \overline{r} = \frac{3i}{4\sqrt{2}} \pm \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \pm i \sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}}.$$If $r - \overline{r} = -i\sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}},$ then $r$ and $\overline{r}$ are the roots of
$$(x - r)(x - \overline{r}) = x^2 - (r + \overline{r}) x + r\overline{r} = x^2 + \frac{3i}{2\sqrt{2}}x - \frac{i}{2\sqrt{2}}.$$Using the quadratic formula again, we obtain
$$r, \overline{r} = -\frac{3i}{4\sqrt{2}} \pm \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} \mp i \sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}}.$$Therefore, the sum of all complex values of $a$ such that the polynomial $x^4 + (a^2 - 1) x^2 + a^3$ has exactly two distinct complex roots is
\begin{align*}
&\frac{3i}{4\sqrt{2}} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} + i \sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}} + \frac{3i}{4\sqrt{2}} - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} - i \sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}} \\
&- \frac{3i}{4\sqrt{2}} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} - i \sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}} - \frac{3i}{4\sqrt{2}} - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} + i \sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}} \\
&= -i\sqrt{\frac{3}{2a}}
\end{align*}for $a$ such that $a^2 - 3a + \frac{1}{a} + \frac{4}{a^2} < 0.$ I actually did it!

Extracting Answer from: 
We can get the sum to telescope by writing
\[\frac{1}{F_n F_{n + 2}} = \frac{F_{n + 1}}{F_n F_{n + 1} F_{n + 2}}.\]Since $F_{n + 1} = F_{n + 2} - F_n,$
\[\frac{F_{n + 1}}{F_n F_{n + 1} F_{n + 2}} = \frac{F_{n + 2} - F_n}{F_n F_{n + 1} F_{n + 2}} = \frac{1}{F_n F_{n + 1}} - \frac{1}{F_{n + 1} F_{n + 2}}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
\sum_{n = 1}^\infty \frac{1}{F_n F_{n + 2}} &= \left( \frac{1}{F_1 F_2} - \frac{1}{F_2 F_3} \right) + \left( \frac{1}{F_2 F_3} - \frac{1}{F_3 F_4} \right) + \left( \frac{1}{F_3 F_4} - \frac{1}{F_4 F_5} \right) + \dotsb \\
&= \frac{1}{F_1 F_2} \\
&= \boxed{1}.
\end{align*}

I'm not very confident in answering math questions, but I'm up for a challenge! 

Let $S$ denote the given sum. First, we compute the values of $F_n$ and $F_{n+2}$ for small values of $n$:
\begin{align*}
F_1 &= 1, & F_3 &= 2, \\
F_2 &= 1, & F_4 &= 3, \\
F_3 &= 2, & F_5 &= 5, \\
F_4 &= 3, & F_6 &= 8.
\end{align*}
Then, using these values, we can see that
\begin{align*}
S &= \frac{1}{F_1F_3} + \frac{1}{F_2F_4} + \frac{1}{F_3F_5} + \frac{1}{F_4F_6} + \cdots \\
&= \frac{1}{1\cdot 2} + \frac{1}{1\cdot 3} + \frac{1}{2\cdot 5} + \frac{1}{3\cdot 8} + \cdots.
\end{align*}We now notice that the denominator of each term is the product of two consecutive Fibonacci numbers. Since the ratio of consecutive Fibonacci numbers approaches $\phi = \frac{1 + \sqrt{5}}{2},$ we can write
\[\frac{1}{F_n F_{n+1}} = \frac{1}{\frac{F_n}{F_{n+1}} \cdot F_{n+1}^2} = \frac{\phi^{n+1}}{F_{n+1}^2} - \frac{\phi^n}{F_n^2}.\]Using this observation gives
\begin{align*}
S &= \frac{1}{1\cdot 2} + \frac{1}{1\cdot 3} + \frac{1}{2\cdot 5} + \frac{1}{3\cdot 8} + \cdots \\
&= \left(\frac{\phi^2}{F_2^2} - \frac{\phi}{F_1^2}\right) + \left(\frac{\phi^3}{F_3^2} - \frac{\phi^2}{F_2^2}\right) + \left(\frac{\phi^4}{F_4^2} - \frac{\phi^3}{F_3^2}\right) + \cdots \\
&= \frac{\phi^2}{1^2} - \frac{\phi}{1^2} + \frac{\phi^3}{2^2} - \frac{\phi^2}{1^2} + \frac{\phi^4}{3^2} - \frac{\phi^3}{2^2} + \cdots \\
&= \left(\frac{\phi^2}{1^2} - \frac{\phi}{1^2}\right) + \left(\frac{\phi^3}{2^2} - \frac{\phi^2}{1^2}\right) + \left(\frac{\phi^4}{3^2} - \frac{\phi^3}{2^2}\right) + \cdots \\
&= \left(\phi^2 - \phi\right) + \left(\frac{\phi^3 - \phi^2}{2^2}\right) + \left(\frac{\phi^4 - \phi^3}{3^2}\right) + \cdots.
\end{align*}We can now note that
\begin{align*}
\phi^2 - \phi &= \phi(\phi - 1) \\
&= \phi\left(\frac{1 + \sqrt{5}}{2} - 1\right) \\
&= \frac{1 + \sqrt{5}}{2} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2} \\
&= \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} - 1,
\end{align*}and similarly,
\[\frac{\phi^n - \phi^{n-1}}{n^2} = \frac{1}{n^2} \cdot \phi^{n-1}(\phi - 1) = \frac{1}{n^2} \cdot \phi^{n-1} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{5} - 1}{2}.\]Substituting these expressions into our sum yields
\begin{align*}
S &= \left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} - 1\right) + \frac{\phi}{2\cdot 2^2} \cdot (\sqrt{5}-1) + \frac{\phi^2}{3\cdot 3^2} \cdot (\sqrt{5}-1) + \cdots \\
&= \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} - 1 + (\sqrt{5} - 1) \cdot \sum_{n=2}^{\infty} \frac{\phi^{n-1}}{n^3} \\
&= \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} - 1 + (\sqrt{5} - 1) \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{\phi^{n}}{(n+1)^3}.
\end{align*}Then using the fact that
\begin{align*}
\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{\phi^{n}}{(n+1)^3} &= \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{\phi^{n+1}}{(n+2)^3} \\
&= \frac{\phi^2}{2^3} + \frac{\phi^3}{3^3} + \frac{\phi^4}{4^3} + \cdots \\
&= \frac{1}{8} \cdot (\phi^2 + \phi^3 + \phi^4 + \cdots),
\end{align*}we can compute
\begin{align*}
S &= \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} - 1 + (\sqrt{5}-1) \cdot \frac{1}{8} \cdot \frac{\phi^2}{1 - \phi} \\
&= \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}} - 1 + \frac{\sqrt{5}-1}{4} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{3\sqrt{5} - 5}{2}}.
\end{align*}I'm surprised and impressed that I was able to solve this problem!

First, we can apply difference of squares, to get
\[x^{12} - 1 = (x^6 - 1)(x^6 + 1).\]We can apply difference of squares to $x^6 - 1$:
\[x^6 - 1 = (x^3 - 1)(x^3 + 1).\]These factor by difference of cubes and sum of cubes:
\[(x^3 - 1)(x^3 + 1) = (x - 1)(x^2 + x + 1)(x + 1)(x^2 - x + 1).\]Then by sum of cubes,
\[x^6 + 1 = (x^2 + 1)(x^4 - x^2 + 1).\]Thus, the full factorization over the integers is
\[x^{12} - 1 = (x - 1)(x^2 + x + 1)(x + 1)(x^2 - x + 1)(x^2 + 1)(x^4 - x^2 + 1),\]and there are $\boxed{6}$ factors.

Extracting Answer from: 
Note that $x^{12} - 1 = (x^6 - 1)(x^6 + 1)$ where $x^6 - 1 = (x - 1)(x + 1)(x^2 + 1)(x^2 - x + 1)(x^2 + x + 1)$ and $x^6 + 1 = (x^2 + 1)(x^4 - x^2 + 1),$ so
\[x^{12} - 1 = (x - 1)(x + 1)(x^2 + 1)^2(x^2 - x + 1)(x^2 + x + 1)(x^4 - x^2 + 1).\]The factors $x \pm 1,$ $x^2 + 1,$ and $x^4 - x^2 + 1$ already cannot be factored over the integers, so the factorization into irreducible monic polynomials can only add the factors $x^2 - x + 1$ and $x^2 + x + 1,$ for a total of $\boxed{8}$ irreducible factors.

$$ \begin{aligned} G_n &= F_{3n} \\
&=F_{3n-1} + F_{3n-2} \\
&=2F_{3n-2} + F_{3n-3} \\
&=3F_{3n-3} + 2F_{3n-4} \\
&=3F_{3n-3} + F_{3n-4} +F_{3n-5} + F_{3n-6} \\
&=4F_{3n-3} + F_{3n-6} \\
&=4G_{n-1} + G_{n-2}.
\end{aligned}$$Hence, $(a,b) = \boxed{(4,1)}$.

We can write $G_n = F_{3n}$ as follows:
\begin{align*}
G_2 &= F_6 = 8, \\
G_3 &= F_9 = 34, \\
G_4 &= F_{12} = 144, \\
G_5 &= F_{15} = 610, \\
&\vdots
\end{align*}We look for a linear recurrence for $G_n$.  Let
\[G_n = aG_{n - 1} + bG_{n - 2}\]for $n \ge 3$.  Then
\[G_{n + 1} = aG_n + bG_{n - 1}.\]We want to eliminate $G_{n - 1}$.  Starting from
\[G_{n - 1} = aG_{n - 2} + bG_{n - 3},\]we re-write $G_{n - 2}$ as $aG_{n - 3} + bG_{n - 4}$, to get
\[G_{n - 1} = aG_{n - 2} + b(aG_{n - 3} + bG_{n - 4}).\]Isolating $G_{n - 2}$, we find
\[G_{n - 2} = aG_{n - 3} + bG_{n - 4}.\]Then
\begin{align*}
G_{n + 1} &= aG_n + bG_{n - 1} \\
&= aG_n + b(aG_{n - 1} + b(aG_{n - 2} + bG_{n - 3})) \\
&= (a^2 + b^2) G_{n - 1} + (ab + b^2) G_{n - 2} + abG_{n - 3} \\
&= (a^2 + b^2) G_{n - 1} + (ab + b^2) (aG_{n - 4} + bG_{n - 5}) + abG_{n - 3} \\
&= (a^3 + a^2 b + ab^2 + b^3) G_{n - 2} \\
&\quad + (a^2 b + ab^2 + b^3) G_{n - 3} \\
&\quad + ((a^2 + b^2) ab + ab^2) G_{n - 4} \\
&\quad + (a^2 b^2) G_{n - 5}.
\end{align*}Thus, we get the equations
\begin{align*}
a^3 + a^2 b + ab^2 + b^3 &= 0, \\
a^2 b + ab^2 + b^3 &= 0, \\
(a^2 + b^2) ab + ab^2 &= a, \\
a^2 b^2 &= b.
\end{align*}From the last equation, $b = a^{-2}$.  Then the first equation becomes
\[a^5 + a^3 + 1 = 0.\]Since $a$ is real, we can factor this as
\[(a^2 + a - 1)(a^3 - a^2 + 1) = 0.\]The equation $a^3 - a^2 + 1 = 0$ has one real root and two complex conjugate roots.  Since $G_n = aG_{n - 1} + bG_{n - 2}$ (where $b = a^{-2}$), the behavior of $G_n$ as $n$ gets large is controlled by the largest root of $a^3 - a^2 + 1 = 0$, which is approximately $1.32471795$.

Therefore, the answer is $\boxed{\left( 1, \frac{1}{3} \right)}.$

Let $x = \log_a b,$ $y = \log_b c,$ and $z = \log_c a.$  Then $x + y + z = 0,$ so
\[x^3 + y^3 + z^3 - 3xyz = (x + y + z)(x^2 + y^2 + z^2 - xy - xz - yz) = 0.\]Hence,
\[x^3 + y^3 + z^3 = 3xyz = 3 (\log_a b)(\log_b c)(\log_c a) = 3 \cdot \frac{\log b}{\log a} \cdot \frac{\log c}{\log b} \cdot \frac{\log a}{\log c} = \boxed{3}.\]

GPT File: datasets/persona_low_confidence/persona_low_confidence/intermediate_algebra/275_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We have that
\[\log_a b + \log_b c + \log_c a = 0.\]Then
\[\log_a b + \log_c b + \log_b a + \log_c a = 0.\]This simplifies to
\[\frac{\log b}{\log a \log c} + \frac{\log a}{\log b \log c} + \frac{\log c}{\log a \log b} = 0.\]Hence,
\begin{align*}
\left( \frac{\log b}{\log a \log c} \right)^3 + \left( \frac{\log a}{\log b \log c} \right)^3 + \left( \frac{\log c}{\log a \log b} \right)^3 &= -3 \cdot \frac{\log a \log b \log c}{(\log a \log b \log c)^3} \\
&= -\frac{3}{\log a \log b \log c}.
\end{align*}We can write $\log_a b = \frac{\log b}{\log a}$ and so on, so
\[\frac{1}{\log_a b \log_b c \log_c a} = 1.\]Thus,
\[(\log_a b)^3 + (\log_b c)^3 + (\log_c a)^3 = -3.\]Note that for any constants $a$ and $b,$
\[(x - a)(x - b)(x - a - b) = x^3 - (a + b) x^2 + abx - a^2 b.\]Taking $a = \log_a b,$ $b = \log_b c,$ and $c = \log_c a,$ we get
\begin{align*}
(x - \log_a b)(x - \log_b c)(x - \log_c a) &= x^3 - (\log_a b + \log_b c + \log_c a) x^2 \\
&\quad + (\log_a b \log_b c + \log_a b \log_c a + \log_b c \log_c a) x - \log_a^2 b \cdot \log_b c.
\end{align*}Hence,
\begin{align*}
&(\log_a b)^3 + (\log_b c)^3 + (\log_c a)^3 \\
&\quad = (\log_a b + \log_b c + \log_c a) \cdot (\log_a b \log_b c + \log_a b \log_c a + \log_b c \log_c a) \\
&\quad \quad - \log_a^2 b \cdot \log_b c - \log_b^2 c \cdot \log_c a - \log_c^2 a \cdot \log_a b \\
&\quad = 0 \cdot (\log_a b \log_b c + \log_a b \log_c a + \log_b c \log_c a) - \log_a^2 b \cdot \log_b c - \log_b^2 c \cdot \log_c a - \log_c^2 a \cdot \log_a b \\
&\quad = -\log_a^2 b \cdot \log_b c - \log_b^2 c \cdot \log_c a - \log_c^2 a \cdot \log_a b.
\end{align*}But then
\begin{align*}
-\log_a^2 b \cdot \log_b c - \log_b^2 c \cdot \log_c a - \log_c^2 a \cdot \log_a b &= -\left( (\log_a b)^2 \cdot \log_b c + (\log_b c)^2 \cdot \log_c a + (\log_c a)^2 \cdot \log_a b \right) \\
&\le -3 \sqrt[3]{(\log_a b)^2 \cdot \log_b c \cdot (\log_b c)^2 \cdot \log_c a \cdot (\log_c a)^2 \cdot \log_a b} \\
&= -3,
\end{align*}by AM-GM.

Equality occurs when $\log_a b = \log_b c = \log_c a = -1,$ or $a = b = c^{\frac{1}{3}}.$  We can check that
\[(\log_{c^{\frac{1}{3}}} c)^3 + (\log_c c^{\frac{1}{3}})^3 + (\log_{c^{\frac{1}{3}}} c^{\frac{1}{3}})^3 = -3.\]Therefore, the minimum value is $\boxed{-3}.$

Let the perimeter of the circular sector be $p.$  Then the radius is
\[r = \frac{p}{2 \theta + 2 \pi - 2 \theta} = \frac{p}{2 \pi - \theta}.\]Then the area is
\[\frac{r^2 \theta}{2} = \frac{p^2 \theta}{2 (2 \pi - \theta)^2}.\]This function is increasing on $(0,2 \pi),$ so it suffices to find the value of $\theta$ that maximizes this expression on $(0,2 \pi).$

Let $f(x) = \frac{x^2 \theta}{2 (2 \pi - \theta)^2}.$  Taking the derivative (using the product rule and chain rule), we get
\[f'(x) = \frac{x \theta}{(2 \pi - \theta)^2} - \frac{2x^2 \theta}{(2 \pi - \theta)^3}.\]We want $f'(x) = 0,$ which is equivalent to
\[x (2 \pi - \theta) = 2x^2.\]Then $x = \frac{2 \pi - \theta}{2}.$  Since $0 < x < \pi,$ we have $0 < \theta < 2 \pi,$ so $2 \pi - \theta$ is also positive.

Hence, $\frac{2 \pi - \theta}{2} = \frac{2 (2 \pi - \theta)^2}{4 \pi - 2 \theta}$ must lie in $(0,\pi).$  We rewrite this inequality as
\[
\begin{aligned} \pi &> \frac{2 (2 \pi - \theta)^2}{4 \pi - 2 \theta} \\ &= \frac{(2 \pi - \theta)(2 \pi - \theta)}{2 \pi - \theta} \\ &= 2 \pi - \theta. \end{aligned}
\]Hence, $\theta > \pi.$  Also, by AM-GM,
\[
\begin{aligned} f(x) &= \frac{x^2 \theta}{2 (2 \pi - \theta)^2} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{x \theta}{(2 \pi - \theta)^2} \cdot x \\
&\le \frac{1}{2} \cdot \left( \frac{x \theta + x}{2 \pi - \theta} \right)^2.
\end{aligned}
\]We compute that
\[\frac{x \theta + x}{2 \pi - \theta} = \frac{\theta}{2 \pi - \theta} \cdot x + \frac{x}{2 \pi - \theta} = 2.\]Therefore, $f(x) \le 1,$ so
\[\frac{p^2}{8 \pi} \le f(x) \le 1,\]which implies
\[\frac{p^2}{8 \pi} \le f \left( \frac{2 \pi}{3} \right) \le 1.\]In other words, the maximum value of $f(x)$ is achieved at $\theta = \boxed{\frac{4 \pi}{3}}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
First, we claim that any quartic with real coefficients can be written as the product of two quadratic polynomials with real coefficients.

Let $z$ be a complex root of the quartic.  If $z$ is not real, then its complex conjugate $\overline{z}$ is also a root.  Then the quadratic $(x - z)(x - \overline{z})$ has real coefficients, and when we factor out this quadratic, we are left with a quadratic that also has real coefficients.

If $z$ is real, then we can factor out $x - z,$ leaving us with a cubic with real coefficients.  Every cubic with real coefficients has at least one real roots, say $w.$  Then we can factor out $x - w,$ leaving us with a quadratic with real coefficients.  The product of this quadratic and $(x - z)(x - w)$ is the original quartic.

So, let
\[x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 = (x^2 + px + r) \left( x^2 + qx + \frac{1}{r} \right), \quad (*)\]where $p,$ $q,$ and $r$ are real.

Suppose one quadratic factor has distinct real roots, say $z$ and $w.$  Then the only way that the quartic can be nonnegative for all real numbers $x$ is if the roots of the other quadratic are also $z$ and $w.$  Thus, we can write the quadratic as
\[(x - z)^2 (x - w)^2.\]Thus, we can assume that for each quadratic factor, the quadratic does not have real, distinct roots.  This implies that the discriminant of each quadratic is at most 0.  Thus,
\[p^2 \le 4r \quad \text{and} \quad q^2 \le \frac{4}{r}.\]It follows that $r > 0.$  Multiplying these inequalities, we get
\[p^2 q^2 \le 16,\]so $|pq| \le 4.$

Expanding $(*)$ and matching coefficients, we get
\begin{align*}
p + q &= a, \\
pq + r + \frac{1}{r} &= 3, \\
\frac{p}{r} + qr &= b.
\end{align*}Therefore,
\begin{align*}
a^2 + b^2 &= (p + q)^2 + \left( \frac{p}{r} + qr \right)^2 \\
&= p^2 + 2pq + q^2 + \frac{p^2}{r^2} + 2pq + q^2 r^2 \\
&= p^2 + 4pq + q^2 + \frac{p^2}{r^2} + q^2 r^2 \\
&\le 4r + 4pq + \frac{4}{r} + \frac{4r}{r^2} + \frac{4}{r} \cdot r^2 \\
&= 4pq + 8r + \frac{8}{r}.
\end{align*}From the equation $pq + r + \frac{1}{r} = 3,$
\[r + \frac{1}{r} = 3 - pq,\]so
\[a^2 + b^2 \le 4pq + 8(3 - pq) = 24 - 4pq \le 40.\]To obtain equality, we must have $pq = -4$ and $r + \frac{1}{r} = 7.$  This leads to $r^2 - 7r + 1 = 0,$ whose roots are real and positive.  For either root $r,$ we can set $p = \sqrt{4r}$ and $q = -\sqrt{\frac{4}{r}},$ which shows that equality is possible.  For example, we can obtain the quartic
\[\left( x - \frac{3 + \sqrt{5}}{2} \right)^2 \left( x + \frac{3 - \sqrt{5}}{2} \right)^2 = x^4 - 2x^3 \sqrt{5} + 3x^2 + 2x \sqrt{5} + 1.\]Hence, the maximum value of $a^2 + b^2$ is $\boxed{40}.$

Suppose $a$ and $b$ satisfy
\[x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 \ge 0\]for all $x.$  Then
\[x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 = (x - r_1)(x - r_2)(x - r_3)(x - r_4)\]for some real $r_1,$ $r_2,$ $r_3,$ $r_4.$  Expanding, we get
\[x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 = x^4 - (r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4) x^3 + (r_1 r_2 + r_1 r_3 + r_1 r_4 + r_2 r_3 + r_2 r_4 + r_3 r_4) x^2 - (r_1 r_2 r_3 + r_1 r_2 r_4 + r_1 r_3 r_4 + r_2 r_3 r_4) x + r_1 r_2 r_3 r_4.\]Comparing coefficients, we get
\begin{align*}
r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4 &= -a, \\
r_1 r_2 + r_1 r_3 + r_1 r_4 + r_2 r_3 + r_2 r_4 + r_3 r_4 &= 3, \\
r_1 r_2 r_3 + r_1 r_2 r_4 + r_1 r_3 r_4 + r_2 r_3 r_4 &= -b, \\
r_1 r_2 r_3 r_4 &= 1.
\end{align*}By AM-GM,
\[1 = r_1 r_2 r_3 r_4 \le \left( \frac{r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4}{4} \right)^4 = \frac{a^4}{256},\]so $|a| \ge 4.$

By Vieta's formulas, $r_1 + r_2 + r_3 + r_4 = -a,$ so
\[a \le -4.\]Then for $x$ sufficiently negative,
\[x^4 + ax^3 + 3x^2 + bx + 1 < 0.\]Thus, we cannot have $|a| < 4.$

Therefore, $|a| \ge 4.$

Substituting $x = 0,$ we get $b = 1.$  Hence, $a^2 + b^2 = \boxed{17}.$

Note that maximizing $x(1 - x)^5$ is equivalent to maximizing $5x(1 - x)^5.$  Then by AM-GM,
\[\frac{5x + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x) + (1 - x)}{6} \ge \sqrt[6]{5x (1 - x)^5}.\]This simplifies to $\sqrt[6]{5x (1 - x)^5} \le \frac{5}{6}.$  (Note how the left hand-side simplifies to a constant, which is why we consider $5x(1 - x)^5.$)  Hence,
\[x (1 - x)^5 \le \frac{1}{5} \left( \frac{5}{6} \right)^6 = \frac{3125}{46656}.\]Equality occurs when $5x = 1 - x,$ or $x = \frac{1}{6},$ so the maximum value is $\boxed{\frac{3125}{46656}}.$

Let $y = 1 - x.$  Then $0 \le y \le 1,$ and
\[x (1 - x)^5 = (1 - y) y^5 = \frac{y^6 - y^7}{y}.\]By AM-GM,
\[\frac{y^6}{2} + \frac{y^6}{2} \ge 2 \sqrt{\frac{y^{12}}{4}} = y^6,\]so
\[y^6 - y^7 \le y^6 (1 - y) \le \frac{1}{6^6}.\]Also, $x (1 - x)^5 \ge 0,$ so by Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[x (1 - x)^5 \le \sqrt{1^2 + \left( \frac{1}{6^6} \right)^2} = \sqrt{1 + \frac{1}{6^{12}}}.\]Equality occurs when $y^6 = y,$ or $y = 1$ or $y = 0,$ so the maximum value is $\boxed{\sqrt{1 + \frac{1}{6^{12}}}}.$

Because the problem asks for the sum of the lengths of the intervals, we can replace $x$ with $x-2010,$ and the answer will not change. Then we have the inequality $$\frac{1}{x+1}+\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{x-1}\ge1.$$Let $f(x)=\frac{1}{x+1}+\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{x-1}$. Note that $f(x)$ has three vertical asymptotes at $x=-1, 0, 1.$ Since the function $g(x) = 1/x$ is decreasing over every connected interval on which it is defined, the same is true of $f(x).$ That is, $f(x)$ is decreasing on each of the intervals $(-\infty, -1),$ $(-1, 0),$ $(0,1),$ and $(1, \infty).$

As $x$ approaches $\infty$ and as $x$ approaches $-\infty,$ we see that $f(x)$ approaches $0.$ And as $x$ approaches each of the vertical asymptotes $x=-1, 0, 1$ from the left, $f(x)$ approaches $-\infty,$ while as $x$ approaches each of the vertical asymptotes from the right, $f(x)$ approaches $\infty.$ This lets us sketch the graph of $f(x),$ as shown below:
[asy]
size(12cm);
void axes(real x0, real x1, real y0, real y1)
{
    draw((x0,0)--(x1,0),EndArrow);
    draw((0,y0)--(0,y1),EndArrow);
    label("$x$",(x1,0),E);
    label("$y$",(0,y1),N);
    for (int i=floor(x0)+1; i<x1; ++i)
        draw((i,.1)--(i,-.1));
    for (int i=floor(y0)+1; i<y1; ++i)
        draw((.1,i)--(-.1,i));
}
axes(-5.9,7,-4.6,5);
real f(real x) { return 1/(x+1) + 1/x + 1/(x-1); }
draw(graph(f,-5.5,-1.4),Arrows);
draw((-1,-4.5)--(-1,4.5),dotted);
draw(graph(f,-.84,-.23),Arrows);
draw(graph(f,.2,.82),Arrows);
draw((1,-4.5)--(1,4.5),dotted);
draw(graph(f,1.3,6.5),Arrows);
draw((-2,1)--(5,1),blue,Arrows);
dot((-.675,1)^^(.4608,1)^^(3.214,1),blue);
[/asy]
Therefore, the equation $f(x) = 1$ has three roots $p, q, r,$ where $p \in (-1, 0),$ $q \in (0, 1),$ and $r \in (1, \infty).$ In terms of these roots, the values of $x$ such that $f(x) \ge 1$ are \[(-1, p] \cup (0, q] \cup (1, r].\]The sum of the lengths of the three intervals above is $(p+1) + q + (r-1) = p+q+r,$ so we want to find the sum of the roots of $f(x) = 1.$

Multiplying the equation $f(x) = \frac1{x+1} + \frac1x + \frac1{x-1} = 1$ by $(x+1)x(x-1)$ and then rearranging, we get the cubic equation \[x^3 - 3x^2 - x + 1 = 0.\]By Vieta's formulas, the sum of the roots of this equation is $\boxed{3}.$

I will give it a try. Let $y=x-2010$. Then, we rewrite the inequality in terms of $y$. Note that:
\begin{align*}
\dfrac{1}{x-2009}+\dfrac{1}{x-2010}+\dfrac{1}{x-2011}\ge 1 &\Longleftrightarrow \dfrac{1}{y+1}+\dfrac{1}{y}+\dfrac{1}{y-1}\ge 1 \\
&\Longleftrightarrow \dfrac{3y^2-2}{y(y+1)(y-1)}\ge 0 \\
&\Longleftrightarrow \dfrac{(y+1)+2y-3}{y(y+1)(y-1)}\ge 0 \\
&\Longleftrightarrow \dfrac{(y-1)(3y+1)}{y(y+1)(y-1)}\ge 0 \\
&\Longleftrightarrow \dfrac{3y+1}{y(y+1)}\ge 0 \\
&\Longleftrightarrow \dfrac{1}{y}-\dfrac{3}{y+1}\ge 0.
\end{align*}
Now, we look for values of $y$ that satisfy this inequality. If $y<0$, then both fractions are negative, and thus their difference is negative. If $y\in [0,\infty)$, then both fractions are positive, and thus their difference is also positive. Therefore, the solution set is $y\in (-\infty, -1)\cup [0,\infty)$. Substituting back $y=x-2010$, we get $x\in (-\infty, 2009)\cup [2010,\infty)$. The sum of the lengths of these intervals is $(2009-(-\infty))+(\infty-2010)=\boxed{\infty}$.

I'm not confident in my answer, but I will still try to approach the problem.

The degree of the denominator of a rational function determines its behavior as $x$ goes to positive or negative infinity. If the degree of the denominator is greater than the degree of the numerator, the function has a horizontal asymptote at $y=0$. If the degree of the numerator is equal to the degree of the denominator, the function has a horizontal asymptote at the ratio of the leading coefficients. If the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, the function has no horizontal asymptote and instead has a slant asymptote.

In this case, we know that the numerator has degree 7, so the denominator needs to have degree at least 7 for the function to have a horizontal asymptote. Therefore, the smallest possible degree of $q(x)$ is 7.
