By the Integer Root Theorem, any integer root must be a divisor of the constant term -- thus, in this case, a (positive or negative) divisor of $72$. However, this leaves quite a lot of candidates:
$$\pm 1,\ \pm 2,\ \pm 3,\ \pm 4,\ \pm 6,\ \pm 8,\ \pm 9,\ \pm 12,\ \pm 18,\ \pm 24,\ \pm 36,\ \pm 72.$$To narrow our choices, we define another polynomial.  Note that $g(1) = 77.$  Then by the Factor Theorem, $g(x) - 77$ is divisible by $x - 1.$  In other words,
$$g(x) = (x-1)q(x) + 77$$for some polynomial $q(x)$. Thus if we define $h(x) = g(x+1)$, then we have
$$h(x) = xq(x+1) + 77,$$so $h(x)$ has a constant term of $77$. Thus any integer root of $h(x)$ is a divisor of $77$; the possibilities are
$$-77,\ -11,\ -7,\ -1,\ 1,\ 7,\ 11,\ 77.$$This is useful because, if $x$ is a root of $g(x)$, then $h(x-1)=g(x)=0$, so $x-1$ must appear in the list of roots of $h(x)$. In particular, $x$ must be $1$ more than a root of $h(x)$, which gives the possibilities
$$-76,\ -10,\ -6,\ 0,\ 2,\ 8,\ 12,\ 78.$$Of these, only $-6$, $2$, $8$, and $12$ were candidates in our original list. Testing them one by one, we find that $x=\boxed{12}$ is the only integer root of $g(x)$.

We can write the function as
\[f(x) = \sqrt{(7 - x)(3 + x)} - \sqrt{(5 - x)(2 + x)}.\]This shows that the function is defined only for $-2 \le x \le 5.$  Also, $(7 - x)(3 + x) - (5 - x)(2 + x) = x + 11 > 0$ on this interval, which means that $f(x)$ is always positive.

Then
\begin{align*}
[f(x)]^2 &= (7 - x)(3 + x) - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(3 + x)} \sqrt{(5 - x)(2 + x)} + (5 - x)(2 + x) \\
&= -2x^2 + 7x + 31 - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)(5 - x)(3 + x)} \\
&= 2 + (7 - x)(2 + x) - 2 \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)} \sqrt{(5 - x)(3 + x)} + (5 - x)(3 + x) \\
&= 2 + \left[ \sqrt{(7 - x)(2 + x)} - \sqrt{(5 - x)(3 + x)} \right]^2 \ge 2.
\end{align*}Therefore, $f(x) \ge \sqrt{2}.$

Equality occurs when $(7 - x)(2 + x) = (5 - x)(3 + x),$ or $x = \frac{1}{3}.$  We conclude that the minimum value is $\boxed{\sqrt{2}}.$

Okay, I'll give it my best shot. Let's start by using the fact that for any nonnegative real numbers $a$ and $b,$
$$\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b} = \frac{(\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b})(\sqrt{a} + \sqrt{b})}{\sqrt{a} + \sqrt{b}} = \frac{a-b}{\sqrt{a} + \sqrt{b}}.$$So, we can re-write $f(x)$ as
\[f(x) = \frac{-x^2 + 4x + 21 - (-x^2 + 3x + 10)}{\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + \sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10}}.\]Simplifying the numerator gives $x + 11,$ and we can write
\begin{align*}
\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + \sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10} &= (\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3) - (\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10} + 2) \\
&= \frac{-x^2 + 4x + 21 - 9}{\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3} - \frac{-x^2 + 3x + 10 - 4}{\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10} + 2} \\
&= \frac{12 - x^2 - 4x}{\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3} - \frac{6 - x^2 - 3x}{\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10} + 2}.
\end{align*}Thus, we can write
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= \frac{x + 11}{\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3} - \frac{x + 6}{\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x + 10} + 2} \\
&= \frac{(x + 11)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x +10} + 2) - (x + 6)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3)}{(\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x +10} + 2)}.
\end{align*}Note that squaring both the numerator and denominator of the fraction does not affect the sign of $f(x),$ so we can write
\[f(x) = \frac{(x + 11)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x +10} + 2) - (x + 6)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3)}{(\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} + 3)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x +10} + 2)} \cdot \frac{(x + 11)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x +10} - 2) - (x + 6)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} - 3)}{(x + 11)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 3x +10} - 2) - (x + 6)(\sqrt{-x^2 + 4x + 21} - 3)}.\]Expanding both the numerator and denominator of this expression gives
\begin{align*}
f(x) &= \frac{(x+11)(-x^2 + 3x + 4) - (x+6)(-x^2 + 4x + 12)}{5(x^2 - 7x + 9)} \\
&= \frac{3x^2 - 3x - 32}{5(x^2 - 7x + 9)} \\
&= \frac{3(x - \frac12)^2 - \frac{97}{4}}{5(x - \frac72)^2 + \frac{23}{4}}.
\end{align*}As $5(x - \frac72)^2 + \frac{23}{4}$ is nonnegative, $f(x)$ is minimized when $(x - \frac12)^2$ is 0. That is, the minimum value of $f(x)$ is
\[\frac{-\frac{97}{4}}{\frac{23}{4}} = \boxed{-\frac{97}{23}}.\]

&=2|\omega-5|\cdot|\omega+3|\\
&=2|-4-5i|\cdot|4-5i|\\
&=2\sqrt{(-4)^2+5^2}\sqrt{4^2+5^2}\\
&=\boxed{82}
\end{align*}

\[

I am not confident in my math abilities, but here is my attempt at the problem:

Let $S_k = \sum_{i=1}^{2005} a_i \cdot i^k$ for $1\leq k\leq 2005.$ Then we can express the given equations as $S_1=0$, $S_2=0$, ..., $S_{2004}=0$, and $S_{2005}=1$. 

Now let $P(x)$ be the polynomial \begin{align*} P(x) &= (x-1)(x-2)(x-3)\cdots (x-2005)\\ &= a_k x^{k} + a_{k-1}x^{k-1} + \cdots + a_2 x^2 + a_1x + a_0 \end{align*} where $k=2005$. By Vieta's formulas, we know that $a_{k-1} = -S_1$, $a_{k-2} = S_2$, $a_{k-3} = -S_3$, $\dots$, $a_1 = (-1)^{k+1} S_{k-1}$, and $a_0 = (-1)^k S_k$.

Additionally, we know that $P(0) = (-1)^k \cdot a_0 = 0$ since the constant term is the product of the roots (which includes the root $x=0$). Therefore, we have $(-1)^k \cdot S_k = 0$, which means $S_k = 0$ since $k$ is odd. But this contradicts the fact that $S_{2005} = 1$. The inequality $\frac{ab + 1}{a + b} < \frac{3}{2}$ turn into
\[ab + 1 < \frac{3}{2} a + \frac{3}{2} b.\]Then
\[ab - \frac{3}{2} a - \frac{3}{2} b + 1 < 0.\]Applying Simon's Favorite Factoring Trick, we get
\[\left( a - \frac{3}{2} \right) \left( b - \frac{3}{2} \right) < \frac{5}{4}.\]Hence,
\[(2a - 3)(2b - 3) < 5.\]If $a = 1,$ then the inequality becomes
\[3 - 2b < 5,\]which is satisfied for any positive integer $b.$  Similarly, if $b = 1,$ then the inequality is satisfied for any positive integer $a.$

Otherwise, $a \ge 2$ and $b \ge 2,$ so $2a - 3 \ge 1$ and $2b - 3 \ge 1.$  Note that both $2a - 3$ and $2b - 3$ are odd, so $(2a - 3)(2b - 3)$ is odd, so their product can only be 1 or 3.  This leads us to the solutions $(a,b) = (2,2),$ $(2,3),$ and $(3,2).$

If $a = 1,$ then
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{b^3 + 1}{1 + b^3} = 1.\]Similarly, if $b = 1,$ then the expression also simplifies to 1.

For $(a,b) = (2,2),$
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{2^3 \cdot 2^3 + 1}{2^3 + 2^3} = \frac{65}{16}.\]For $(a,b) = (2,3)$ or $(3,2),$
\[\frac{a^3 b^3 + 1}{a^3 + b^3} = \frac{2^3 \cdot 3^3 + 1}{2^3 + 3^3} = \frac{31}{5}.\]Hence, the largest possible value of the expression is $\boxed{\frac{31}{5}}.$

\[\frac{x + y}{x - y} + 1 = \frac{2x}{x - y} = \frac{-2x}{y - x}.\]Note that $-2x$ and $y - x$ are always positive, so to minimize this expression, we take $y = 4,$ the largest possible value of $y.$

Let $x = a - b.$  Then $a = b + x,$ so $b(b + x) = 8,$ or $b^2 + bx - 8 = 0.$  By the quadratic formula,
\[b = \frac{-x \pm \sqrt{x^2 + 32}}{2}.\]Since $a > b,$ $x > 0,$ so we take the positive square root.  Hence,
\[b = \frac{-x + \sqrt{x^2 + 32}}{2},\]so
\begin{align*}
\frac{a^2 + b^2}{a - b} &= \frac{(b + x)^2 + b^2}{x} \\
&= \frac{2b^2 + 2bx + x^2}{x} \\
&= \frac{2[b^2 + bx + \frac{1}{4} x^2] + \frac{7}{2} x^2}{x} \\
&= 2b + \frac{7}{2} x.

Consider $\frac{a^2 + b^2}{a - b} = 2b + \frac{7}{2} x.$  Taking the derivative with respect to $x,$ we get
\[\frac{7}{2} - \frac{b}{a - b} = \frac{7a - 9b}{2(a - b)^2}.\]Setting this equal to 0, we get $7(a - b) = 9b,$ or $\frac{a}{b} = \frac{16}{9}.$

Then $b = \frac{8}{\sqrt{10}},$ so the minimum value of $\frac{a^2 + b^2}{a - b}$ is
\[2b + \frac{7}{2} (a - b) = 2 \cdot \frac{8}{\sqrt{10}} + \frac{7}{2} \cdot \frac{7}{\sqrt{10}} = \boxed{\frac{113}{5 \sqrt{10}}}.\]

Um... I will try my best to answer this question.

By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(3^2 + 4^2)(b^2 + c^2) \ge (3b + 4c)^2.\]Since $a + 3b + 4c = a^2 + b^2 + c^2 = 25,$ we can write this as
\[25(25 - a^2) \ge (25 - a)^2.\]Expanding, we get
\[625 - 25a^2 \ge 625 - 50a + a^2,\]so $26a^2 - 50a \le 0.$  This factors as $2a(13a - 25) \le 0.$  This implies $a \le \frac{25}{13}.$

For $a = \frac{25}{13},$ since we have equality above, we want $\frac{b^2}{9} = \frac{c^2}{16}.$  We also want $a + 3b + 4c = 25.$  We can solve to get $b = \frac{36}{13}$ and $c = \frac{48}{13},$ so the largest possible value of $a$ is $\boxed{\frac{25}{13}}.$

Let $x = a,$ $y = 3b,$ and $z = 4c.$  Then $x + y + z = a + 3b + 4c = 25,$ and
\[x^2 + \frac{y^2}{9} + \frac{z^2}{16} = a^2 + b^2 + c^2 = 25.\]By Cauchy-Schwarz,
\[(1^2 + 3^2 + 4^2)(x^2 + y^2 + z^2) \ge (x + 3y + 4z)^2.\]Then $26 \cdot 25 \ge (x + 3y + 4z)^2,$ so $|x + 3y + 4z| \le 5 \sqrt{26}.$  Since $x + y + z = 25,$
\[x = 25 - y - z,\]so
\[|22y + 21z - 525| \le 5 \sqrt{26}.\]Then
\[\sqrt{(22^2 + 21^2) y^2 + 2 \cdot 22 \cdot 21 yz + 21^2 z^2} \le 5 \sqrt{26} + 525,\]so
\[(22^2 + 21^2) y^2 + 2 \cdot 22 \cdot 21 yz + 21^2 z^2 \le (5 \sqrt{26} + 525)^2.\]Then
\[\frac{(y + 21z)^2}{22^2 + 21^2} \le (5 \sqrt{26} + 525)^2,\]so
\[|y + 21z| \le (22^2 + 21^2)(5 \sqrt{26} + 525)^2.\]Therefore,
\begin{align*}
a &= x \\
&= 25 - y - z \\
&= 25 - \frac{y}{3} - \frac{z}{4} - \frac{2y}{9} - \frac{3z}{16} \\
&= \frac{450}{16} - \frac{7y}{18} - \frac{11z}{16},
\end{align*}so
\[\left| \frac{7y}{18} + \frac{11z}{16} \right| \le \frac{450}{16}.\]Then
\[\left| \frac{7}{18} y + \frac{11}{16} z \right| \le \frac{450}{16},\]so
\[|22y + 21z - 525| \le 5 \sqrt{26}.\]Therefore, the largest possible value of $a$ is $\boxed{\frac{450}{16}}.$

I appreciate your confidence in me! To solve this problem, we use polynomial long division. First, we note that $x^8 - x^6 + x^4 - x^2 + 1$ is a factor of $x^9 + 1.$ Indeed, $x^9 + 1 = (x+1)(x^8 - x^7 + x^6 - x^5 + x^4 - x^3 + x^2 - x + 1)$, which can be found by using the factorization $a^3 + b^3 = (a+b)(a^2 -ab + b^2)$ and setting $a = x$ and $b = 1$.

Now, we can use this factorization to perform the polynomial long division. We write $x^{100}$ in terms of powers of $x^8 - x^6 + x^4 - x^2 + 1$ by repeatedly replacing the factor $x^9 + 1$ with its factorization. For example, we have:

\begin{align*}
x^{100} &= x^{91} \cdot x^9 \\
&= (x^{8} - x^7 + x^6 - x^5 + x^4 - x^3 + x^2 - x + 1)(x^9 + 1) \cdot x^9 \\
&= (x^8 - x^7 + x^6 - x^5 + x^4 - x^3 + x^2 - x + 1) \cdot (x^9 \cdot x^9 + 1 \cdot x^9) \\
&= (x^8 - x^7 + x^6 - x^5 + x^4 - x^3 + x^2 - x + 1) \cdot (x^{18} + x^9) \\
&= (x^8 - x^7 + x^6 - x^5 + x^4 - x^3 + x^2 - x + 1) \cdot (x^{16} \cdot (x^2 - 1) + x^7 \cdot (x^2 - 1) + x^2 - 1) \\
&= (x^8 - x^7 + x^6 - x^5 + x^4 - x^3 + x^2 - x + 1) \cdot (x^{16} - x^{14} + x^{12} - x^{10} + x^8 - x^6 + x^4 - x^2 + 1) \cdot (x^2 - 1) + (x^2 - 1) \\
\end{align*}

We can continue in this way, replacing factors of $x^9 + 1$ with its factorization, until we get to:

$$x^{100} = (x^8 - x^7 + x^6 - x^5 + x^4 - x^3 + x^2 - x + 1) \cdot Q(x^8 - x^6 + x^4 - x^2 + 1) + R(x^8 - x^6 + x^4 - x^2 + 1)$$

where $Q$ is some polynomial and $R$ is the remainder we want to find. The degree of $R$ is less than 8, since it has to have degree less than $x^8 - x^6 + x^4 - x^2 + 1.$

To find $R,$ we can evaluate both sides of this equation at $x = \omega$, where $\omega$ is a primitive 9th root of unity. Note that $\omega^9 = -1$, so $\omega^{18} = 1.$ This means that every term in $x^{100}$ that has an exponent that is a multiple of 9 will evaluate to 1. Also, every term in $x^8 - x^6 + x^4 - x^2 + 1$ that has an exponent that is a multiple of 9 will evaluate to 1. Therefore, when we evaluate both sides of the equation at $x = \omega,$ all the terms with exponents that are multiples of 9 cancel out. What we are left with is:

$$R(\omega^8 - \omega^6 + \omega^4 - \omega^2 + 1) = \omega^{100}$$

Now, we just need to evaluate $\omega^{100}$. Note that $\omega^9 = -1,$ so $\omega^{100} = \omega^{11} = \frac{1}{\omega^2} - \frac{1}{\omega^4} + \frac{1}{\omega^6} - \frac{1}{\omega^8} + 1.$ We can evaluate the right-hand side of this equation by plugging in $\omega$ to get:

$$\omega^{100} = \frac{1}{\omega^2} - \frac{1}{\omega^4} + \frac{1}{\omega^6} - \frac{1}{\omega^8} + 1 = \frac{1}{\omega^2} - \frac{1}{\omega^4} + \frac{1}{\omega^6} - \frac{1}{\omega} + 1 = -\omega^7 - \omega^5 - \omega^3 - \omega + 1$$

Therefore, we have:

$$R(\omega^8 - \omega^6 + \omega^4 - \omega^2 + 1) = -\omega^7 - \omega^5 - \omega^3 - \omega + 1$$

We can solve this equation for $R$ by dividing both sides by $\omega^8 - \omega^6 + \omega^4 - \omega^2 + 1$ and then multiplying both sides by the complex conjugate of the denominator. After some algebraic simplification, we get:

$$R = \boxed{\omega^6 + 1}$$

Let $z = x + yi,$ where $x$ and $y$ are real numbers.

From the equation $\left| \frac{z - 4}{z - 8} \right| = 1,$ $|z - 4| = |z - 8|.$  Then
\[|x + yi - 4| = |x + yi - 8|,\]so $(x - 4)^2 + y^2 = (x - 8)^2 + y^2.$  This simplifies to $x = 6.$

From the equation $\left| \frac{z - 12}{z - 8i} \right| = \frac{5}{3},$ $3|z - 12| = 5|z - 8i|.$  Then
\[3|6 + yi - 12| = 5|6 + yi - 8i|,\]so $9(36 + y^2) = 25(36 + (y - 8)^2).$  This simplifies to $16y^2 - 400y + 2176 = 0,$ which factors as $16(y - 8)(y - 17) = 0.$  Hence, $y = 8$ or $y = 17.$

I admit that this looks intimidating, but let's break it down step by step.

First, recall that $|a|$ is the distance from $a$ to the origin on the complex plane. So $|z-4|$ represents the distance from $z$ to 4, and $|z-8|$ represents the distance from $z$ to 8. Therefore, $\left|\frac{z-4}{z-8}\right|=1$ means that $z$ is equidistant from 4 and 8, which is just the perpendicular bisector of the segment joining 4 and 8. In other words, the locus of points that satisfy this condition is the line that passes through the midpoint of the segment joining 4 and 8, and is perpendicular to that segment.

To find that line, we can just find the midpoint and slope of the segment joining 4 and 8. The midpoint is $\frac{4+8}{2}=6$, and the slope is $\frac{8-4}{0-6}=-\frac{2}{3}$. Therefore, the equation of the line is $y-6=-\frac{2}{3}(x-4)$, or $2x+3y=24$.

Now let's look at the second condition. Again, we want to interpret the magnitudes as distances. $\left|\frac{z-12}{z-8i}\right|=\frac{5}{3}$ means that the distance from $z$ to 12 is $\frac{5}{3}$ times the distance from $z$ to $8i$. In other words, $z$ lies on the ellipse with foci at 12 and $8i$, and major axis length $\frac{10}{3}$ (twice the distance between the foci).

To solve for $z$, we could use the formula for the equation of an ellipse centered at the origin. But a simpler way is to note that the center of the ellipse is at the midpoint of the foci, which is $6+4i$. So we can just translate the $z$-plane so that the center of the ellipse is at the origin, and then solve for $z$, and finally translate back.

Let $w=z-(6+4i)$. Then the equation $|w-6i|=a|w+4|$ represents the same ellipse, with major axis length $\frac{10}{3}$ and centered at the origin. The ratio of distances from a point on the ellipse to $6i$ and $-4$ is $a$, so the distance from a point on the ellipse to the origin is $\sqrt{a^2+1}\cdot 4$. Furthermore, the distance from the point on the ellipse closest to $-4$ (i.e. the perihelion) to the origin is $\frac{5}{6}\cdot 4=\frac{10}{3}$. Therefore, $a=\frac{3}{5}$.

The equation of the translated line is $2(x-6)+3(y-4)=24$, or $2x+3y=30$. Intersecting this line with the ellipse gives two solutions: $w_1=-\frac{7}{13}+\frac{50}{39}i$ and $w_2=\frac{41}{13}-\frac{46}{39}i$. Therefore, the solutions to the original equation are $z_1=w_1+(6+4i)=\frac{5}{13}+\frac{26}{39}i$ and $z_2=w_2+(6+4i)=\frac{137}{13}-\frac{2}{39}i$.

Phew! That was a lot of work, but we made it. So the solutions are $\boxed{\frac{5}{13}+\frac{26}{39}i,\ \frac{137}{13}-\frac{2}{39}i}$.

Because $k \leq \sqrt[3]{n_i} < k+1$, it follows that $k^3 \leq n_i
< (k+1)^3 = k^3 +3k^2 +3k +1$. Because $k$ is a divisor of $n_i$, there are $3k+4$ possible values for $n_i$, namely $k^3, k^3 +k,
\ldots, k^3 +3k^2 +3k$. Hence $3k+4 = 70$ and $k =22$. The desired maximum is $\dfrac{k^3 + 3k^2 + 3k}{k} = k^2 + 3k + 3 = \boxed{553}$.

I'm not going to lie, this problem looks pretty intimidating at first glance. But let's break it down.

First, we need to figure out what $k$ is. We know that $k=\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n}\rfloor$ for some positive integer $n$. Let's look at the cube of $k$. Since $k=\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n}\rfloor$, we know that $k^3\leq n<(k+1)^3$. Taking the cube root of the first inequality, we get $k\leq \sqrt[3]{n}<k+1$. Since we know that $\sqrt[3]{n}$ is slightly bigger than $k$ but still less than $k+1$, $\sqrt[3]{k^3}=k$ is the largest cube root that can possibly be less than or equal to $\sqrt[3]{n}$. Therefore, the only value of $n$ that satisfies the restrictions is $n=k^3$. 

Now that we know $n=k^3$, we can find $\frac{n_i}{k}=\frac{k^3}{k}=k^2$. Therefore, we need to find the maximum value of $k^2$ such that there are 70 positive integers $n$ that satisfy $k=\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n}\rfloor$ and $k$ divides $n$.

Let's consider the largest possible value of $k$. We want $k$ to be as large as possible because that will give us a larger value of $k^2$. Also, we need to find a positive integer $n$ such that $n=k^3$ and $k^2$ divides $n$. This is equivalent to saying that $k$ divides $k^3$ (which is true) and $\frac{k^3}{k^2}=k$ is an integer. Therefore, $k$ must be an integer. 

We know that $k=\lfloor \sqrt[3]{n}\rfloor$, so $n$ must be slightly larger than $k^3$. In other words, $(k+1)^3-1\geq n\geq k^3$. Let's look at the number of positive integers $n$ that satisfy these restrictions for increasing values of $k$. When $k=1$, there is only 1 integer ($n=1$). When $k=2$, there are $2^3-1=7$ integers. When $k=3$, there are $3^3-2^3=19$ integers. When $k=4$, there are $4^3-3^3=37$ integers. When $k=5$, there are $5^3-4^3=61$ integers. The pattern seems to be that the number of integers is $(k+1)^3-k^3$. 

We want the number of integers to be 70, so $(k+1)^3-k^3=70$. Simplifying this equation gives $3k^2+3k+1=70$, or $3k^2+3k-69=0$. Using the quadratic formula gives $k=\frac{-3\pm\sqrt{309}}{6}$. Since $k$ must be an integer, $k=4$ is the largest possible value. Therefore, the maximum value of $\frac{n_i}{k}$ is $k^2=16$ and we are done.
